Crude Aid: As Global Oil Demand Tightens, A Big Producer Has Own Agenda --- Venezuela's President Chavez Uses PDVSA to Help Poor, But Output...
Crude Aid: As Global Oil Demand Tightens, A Big Producer Has Own Agenda --- Venezuela's President
Chavez Uses PDVSA to Help Poor, But Output Diminishes --- Free Medical Care in the Slums
By David Luhnow and Peter Millard
2,294 words
1 August 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CARACAS, Venezuela -- Ricardo Coronado, the head of western operations for Venezuela's state-run oil giant,
found out the hard way that his job wasn't just overseeing development of one of the world's richest oil regions.
On a recent Saturday, Venezuela's flamboyant leader Hugo Chavez scolded him on national television for failing
to appear at a ribbon-cutting ceremony for a school in western Venezuela paid for by Petroleos de Venezuela SA.
"The time is past when the president turned up and PDVSA was off somewhere minding its own business," said
Mr. Chavez, who ordered an aide to summon Mr. Coronado. When the hapless executive arrived a half-hour
later, Mr. Chavez was still raging, and cut him off before he could defend himself. "There's nothing you can say,"
the Venezuelan president lectured.
Since Mr. Chavez took power in 1999, he has become PDVSA's de facto CEO, steering the oil company into
political, economic and philanthropic ventures that have distracted it from its core business of finding and
producing more oil. The consequences for PDVSA are stark: Output has fallen to an estimated 1.6 million barrels
a day from nearly 3 million barrels in 1998.
The oil company, the world's third-biggest by most measures, is run along social and political guidelines as much
as business tenets. As a result, much of the decision-making involves figuring out new ways to fund Mr. Chavez's
pet projects. One of the latest ventures was paying to televise soccer's World Cup for free in Bolivia, a Chavez
ally.
Mr. Chavez's geopolitical considerations, and his anti-American bent, also influence the way the company does
business. PDVSA has turned away from traditional partners like U.S. major Exxon Mobil Corp. and is doing much
more business with state companies from Iran, China and India. This weekend, during a visit to Tehran by Mr.
Chavez, Iran pledged to invest $4 billion in two Venezuelan oil fields. The two nations also unveiled a raft of joint
ventures, including a refinery in Indonesia.
PDVSA, however, has announced deals and projects in the past that have so far failed to get off the ground,
including a proposed South American pipeline.
The company's diminished production has cut world output by more than 1%. That may not sound like a lot, but in
a global oil market stretched tight by growing demand, political volatility and hard-to-expand supply, the
company's production shortfall has contributed to the run-up in oil prices during the past few years, and is likely to
continue to do so.
A recent report by the U.S. Government Accountability Office says that PDVSA's long-term decline in output is a
growing energy-security problem for the U.S., which relies on the Andean country as its fourth-biggest oil supplier.
Venezuela boasts the biggest oil reserves outside the Middle East, and is much closer to the U.S. market.
Venezuela's production could slide further. Under recent changes by Mr. Chavez, PDVSA will now be in charge of
managing the privately run fields in Venezuela, where growing production accounts for roughly a third of the
country's total oil output. Mr. Chavez is imposing new rules on the ventures that could hamper their continued
growth, such as requiring them to spend part of their budget on social projects.
Nothing is more central to Mr. Chavez's political fortunes than PDVSA, pronounced peh-deh-VEH-za. The oil
giant is the source of the money his government lavishes on the poor and, in many ways, his 60% approval

--- Page 98 ---
Page 98 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
ratings. Before the former paratrooper took power, many in Venezuela viewed the company as a fiefdom where
oil engineers and technocrats lived comfortably and little was done for the poor.
Under Mr. Chavez, the priorities of PDVSA, which has funded half of government spending for decades, have
changed dramatically. The company still hands over a portion of its profits to the government, but it also gets
directly involved in poverty alleviation and economic development.
The company must spend at least 10% of its annual investment budget on social programs worth about $1 billion
a year. But that figure doesn't include other spending by the oil giant on projects such as building roads and the
government's subsidized food program. That kind of economic aid totaled $8 billion last year alone, the company
says. Palmaven, the PDVSA unit that oversees social spending, is the company's fastest-growing division.
PDVSA's social focus sets it apart from most oil companies, which try to maximize output and profits. Even most
state-run oil companies, which tend to have bloated payrolls, try to mimic private-sector efficiency and focus
entirely on the oil business. PDVSA's shift recalls the role that Mexico's state firm Pemex played for decades in
trying to spur economic development by providing jobs and building roads.
Such attention to economic development, however, gives the company less time and money to devote to its oil
business. It spent just $60 million on exploration in 2004, compared with $174 million in 2001, according to the
company's recently published 2004 financial results. That's bad news for Venezuela, where current wells are so
old that their output falls at an average rate of 23% a year, forcing the company to drill new wells just to keep
production steady.
"The oil industry needs investments in maintenance and expansions," says Domingo Maza Zavala, a central bank
director who applauds the company's social mission but also says it should be more efficient.
In a recent report, Gersan Zurita, an oil analyst at Fitch credit-ratings agency, said that PDVSA's "forced
disinvestment" for social programs has hamstrung the company's ability to produce oil.
Some of PDVSA's problems can be traced to a devastating strike in late 2002 and early 2003, during which Mr.
Chavez sacked about 19,000 employees who opposed his policies. Even though employment is now back to
pre-strike levels, output -- which began to recover in the months after the strike -- has since fallen again,
suggesting a long-term decline at the company.
PDVSA counters that the company is performing well, and says its daily output is 2.2 million barrels, though most
independent analysts and the U.S. government put it at about 1.6 million. The company says it plans to boost
production to 4 million barrels a day by 2012. Venezuelan officials also point out that the country's oil exports to
the U.S. have remained steady since 2004 at 1.5 million barrels a day.
In some cases, Mr. Chavez has literally taken PDVSA assets and handed them to the poor. The elegant five-story
headquarters for PDVSA Servicios, a subsidiary that oversaw communications and technology services for the oil
giant, has been turned into the Bolivarian University of Venezuela. The university's 5,000 students get a free ride:
tuition, materials, health care and food are paid for by the oil company.
Students and teachers view the campus's marble-lined elevators, expensive artwork and baseball field as
evidence that PDVSA's executives lived too cushy a life for a poor nation before Mr. Chavez came to power. As a
group of students and teachers play baseball, 36-year-old English teacher Claire Bendahan looks on in approval.
"This is socialism in action," she says. "Now our country's oil money is being used for the poor."
At this new PDVSA, job creation -- outside the oil business -- is also in. The company is kicking off a new program
to create about 73,000 jobs, including at a paper plant and in ecotourism.
The firm's own hiring is based on social and political goals as much as on talent. Under a new job-placement
system, candidates with larger families are given priority because they are viewed as needing jobs more. Those
who have been unemployed for longer are pushed to the front of the line, too. This goes for technical and
engineering jobs as well as secretarial ones.
Quality and safety have suffered since the strike. Industry executives say PDVSA has botched a number of wells
over the past year due to human error. Contractors complain they have to repeatedly send staffing requests
before getting properly trained oil hands. Even the National Assembly, where every legislator is from a
pro-Chavez party, is alarmed enough that it is investigating whether the new hiring system has excluded
more-experienced workers.

--- Page 99 ---
Page 99 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Company officials say accidents are in decline compared with 2003, just after the strike. "At first we had some
bad stumbles, but the safety side has been improving a lot," says Jorge Luis Sanchez, the head of Venezuela's
Enegas natural gas regulatory agency.
Nowhere has the company's decay turned up faster than at PDVSA's massive 1.3 million-barrel-a-day domestic
refining network, which suffered more than a dozen plant outages in 2005. In March, two workers died in a blast
at the Amuay refinery, the nation's largest. Last month, another explosion and subsequent fire caused extensive
damage at a 190,000-barrel-a-day unit at Amuay, sending spot gasoline prices higher in the U.S. Gulf Coast
where Amuay ships much of its production.
"Workers are scared," says Oswaldo Caibett, the president of the Fedepetrol oil union. The union filed a
complaint with the attorney general's office last year after a deadly refinery fire, accusing management of
negligence.
PDVSA refining chief Alejandro Granado recently acknowledged to the local press that accidents at the refining
complex are above international standards, and that the company had contracted U.S. chemicals firm DuPont Co.
to help it improve safety.
PDVSA's ambivalence about boosting oil production is seen in the shallow waters of the remote Gulf of Paria,
where Christopher Columbus first set foot on the South American continent in 1498. U.S. oil giant ConocoPhillips
discovered the Corocoro oil field in the area in 1999, and spent the next few years drilling exploratory wells and
drawing up a production plan.
But when Conoco was ready to transport a U.S.-made drilling platform to Corocoro in late 2004 to get commercial
production started, PDVSA, which has a 35% stake in the project, refused and insisted the company use a locally
made platform. That decision set back commercial production by more than a year to early 2007.
"Nowhere else in the world do you have a field with 500 million barrels that just sits there," said an oil executive
involved in the project.
PDVSA says that it doesn't want to rely on imported technology and wants to spawn a domestic oil-services
industry to compete with the likes of France's Schlumberger Ltd. and U.S.-based Baker Hughes Inc. PDVSA
recently announced that any private oil company doing business with the state firm must source a majority of
supplies and services in Venezuela.
In the past two years, Mr. Chavez has rewritten the rules of Venezuela's oil industry. In 2004, his government
claimed a majority stake in some 32 operating agreements between private companies and PDVSA, which gave
the state firm at least a 60% stake in most ventures. This year, he announced similar rules for four heavy-oil
projects in the Orinoco region, and is expected to eventually seize operations from two other private ventures not
included in the original 32 deals -- among them the Corocoro field.
Soon, any private oil company working in a joint venture with the state firm will be required to spend 3.3% of its
local investment budget on social programs. "It's not easy. . . . But there will be no more projects with their backs
turned to our reality," Rafael Ramirez, president of PDVSA, told an audience of industry executives in announcing
the move in June.
Oil-service companies, which provide platforms, rigs and drilling supplies, must also include a community project,
such as low-income housing, as part of their bids for contracts from PDVSA. In addition, the companies must
spend up to 5% of the value of each contract hiring local worker-owned service companies to do some of the
work.
Some in the oil service industry are pushing back. Todco, a U.S.-based rig supplier, has already sent two of its
eight Venezuela rigs back to Houston.
Nevertheless, PDVSA's priorities are focused on bettering the lives of people in the nation's teeming slums. In the
seaside town of Catia la Mar, about a half hour drive from the capital, the firm has just built one of 1,000 new
community centers it plans to construct across the country. The centers offer living quarters for two doctors, often
Cubans, who give locals free medical care. The waiting rooms all have new TVs and DVD players.
A local PDVSA employee organizes residents into a dozen committees, including health, sports and political
ideology, which identify local needs and draw up proposals for PDVSA to fund.
The spending goes down well with Yoselin Escobar, a 33-year-old teacher and member of the local education
committee, who is trying to convince the oil company to pay a monthly stipend to stay-at-home mothers who look

--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
after the children of other working moms. "Thanks to our president, the oil company helps us," she says. "I'm
voting for [Chavez] until I die."
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Venezuela's Black Gold" -- WSJ Aug. 18, 2006)

Less Rate Fear: Dow Industrials Rally 119 Points --- Slower-Than-Expected Growth Is Interpreted as Positive News; 10-Year Yield Drops Under...
Friday's Markets
Less Rate Fear: Dow Industrials Rally 119 Points --- Slower-Than-Expected Growth Is Interpreted as
Positive News; 10-Year Yield Drops Under 5%
By E.S. Browning
779 words
29 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HOPES AGAIN returned for an end to Federal Reserve interest-rate increases, and the Dow Jones Industrial
Average enjoyed its strongest weekly gain since May 2005.
Stocks surged Friday after the Commerce Department said the economy grew at a 2.5% annual rate in the
second quarter, less than half the first-quarter rate of 5.6%. The signs of slower growth fueled hopes that the
Federal Reserve will be able to end its two-year campaign of interest-rate increases, which was aimed at cooling
the economy and preventing inflation.
An end to rate increases would lift a cloud from the corporate profit outlook, and that would be good for stocks.
Of course, a sharp slowdown in economic growth also could hurt profits. Some investors also worried about
unexpectedly strong inflation numbers in the report, which could force the Fed to keep raising rates despite
slower growth -- the worst of both worlds. But that didn't hold the market back on Friday.
The Dow industrials jumped 119.27 points, or 1.07%, to 11219.70, up 4.7% for 2006. Stocks had risen early in the
week on hopes for strong quarterly profit reports, and the Dow finished ahead 3.2% on the week, at a three-week
high.
The expectation that interest rates are heading lower in a softer economy also sent bond prices higher, which
pushed yields lower. The yield of the 10-year Treasury note fell below 5% for the first time in more than a month.
For those who fear that slower growth could foreshadow recession, the bond market offered reassuring news. A
widely followed indicator of recession fears gave off an upbeat signal.
The yield of the two-year Treasury fell more sharply than that of the 10-year note, leaving the two-year note with a
slightly lower yield than the 10-year. The two-year note yielded 4.988% late Friday, while the 10-year note yielded
4.996%.
Recently, the opposite has been the case. Longer-term government securities have been carrying lower yields
than shorter-term securities, which worried some investors. Lower rates for longer-term debt can be a sign that
investors fear economic trouble in the future, which would send rates sharply lower. The bond market's renewed
willingness to put a higher interest rate on longer-term government debt suggested that the soft growth numbers
weren't creating many recession fears.
A quieter economy also would mean less oil demand, and oil futures fell $1.30 a barrel to $73.24.
"There is some feeling that the economy is slowing down but it isn't falling off a cliff, so that should bode pretty
well for corporate profits," said Todd Clark, director of stock trading at San Francisco money-management and
brokerage firm Nollenberger Capital Partners.
Another reason for declining oil futures and rising stock prices was a renewed hope that Middle East fighting will
avoid spilling over into other countries. That hope was bolstered by reports that Secretary of State Condoleezza
Rice would be returning to the Middle East. Some investors who had pulled back from stocks on fears of more
Middle East turmoil were gradually returning to stocks last week.

--- Page 104 ---
Page 104 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There was one sign of concern in the market. Gold futures, which sometimes go up when investors are worried
about the future, rose $2.30 to $634.80.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 1.22%, or 15.35 points, to 1278.55, now up 2.4% this year. Its weekly
gain of 3.1% was the sharpest since November of 2004. The Nasdaq Composite Index soared 1.93%, or 39.67
points, to 2094.14, a gain of 3.7% on the week. But its beleaguered technology stocks have been sagging for
most of the year, and the Nasdaq composite remained down 5% for 2006.
Small stocks, which had been under fire since spring, were among the day's biggest gainers. The Russell 2000
small-stock index spurted ahead 2.09%, or 14.34 points, to 700.03 on Friday, now up 4% this year.
The 10-year Treasury note rose 12/32, or $3.75 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield down to 4.996%. The
30-year bond was up 17/32 to yield 5.075%.
The dollar fell to a two-week low on the euro and the yen. Year-to-date, the dollar is down 7.1% against the euro
and is off 2.7% against the yen.

Destinations: Beating Las Vegas --- Nevada's 'other' towns seek life beyond casinos -- ditching poker for cowboys, golf and heli-skiing
Travel
PURSUITS
Destinations: Beating Las Vegas --- Nevada's 'other' towns seek life beyond casinos -- ditching poker for
cowboys, golf and heli-skiing
By Candace Jackson
1,046 words
29 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
P4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Elko, Nev. -- THE LUSH HIKING trails are punctuated with towering Aspens, their trunks carved with the names
of settlers from the Basque region of Spain and France more than 100 years ago. Here in this glacier-carved
canyon, fishermen cast lures for trout in the rushing waterways. But just 25 miles down the road, the call of birds
is replaced by a much more urban sound: the ringing of the poker machines in downtown casinos that form the
backbone of the main street.
Elko is one of Nevada's oldest gambling destinations and, much like Las Vegas, it's on a building boom. But
instead of throwing up casinos and hotels, town officials and entrepreneurs are improving mountain trails
(complete with GPS markers that guide trekkers through miles of wilderness), launching adventure-travel
conventions for snowmobile enthusiasts and opening private ranches to the public so tourists can experience the
buckaroo life firsthand.
In doing so, Elko is in the same boat as many other Nevada towns. With Vegas the big destination for casinos,
visitors often don't want to come to smaller towns for the gambling alone. So these towns are reinventing
themselves, with developers trying everything from building luxury golf courses in Mesquite to converting old
casinos into high-end urban condos in Reno.
Elko is emerging as a model for Nevada's "other" towns. It has one of the country's biggest Basque communities
per capita in the U.S., many of whose members moved there for ranching -- bringing with them a unique take on
the cowboy lifestyle as well as a rich culinary tradition. With the nearby Ruby Mountains fed by many lakes and
streams, wild animals such as elk, mule deer and bighorn sheep make for some of the best hunting in the region.
And the green but rocky mountain terrain is drawing adventurous tourists for sports like heli-skiing in winter and
ATV riding in summer.
One sign of change here is the end of charter flights that used to bring gamblers in from other cities. These
planes flew in every day to Elko Regional Airport in the 1980s. Travelers had to prove their dedication even
before boarding -- opening their wallets to show they had at least $350 in cash to cover the trip and time at the
gambling tables. Until a few months ago, many of Elko's remaining loyalists, mostly retirees looking for a cheap
gambling getaway, still flew in on these flights.
BUT THE LURE of Vegas, along with Indian reservation gaming options near almost every major city and fuel
prices too high to make the increasingly empty charters profitable, brought an end to the flights in February.
On the plus side, the local economy got a recent boost from the gold industry. Five major gold mines are in Elko
County and with prices recently hitting a 26-year high, mining companies are investing more in the area and
bringing high-paying jobs to town. Locals also have more cash to spend. Lida Gammel, owner of one of the local
legal brothels, Mona's Ranch -- which is visited by everyone from miners to cowboys and tourists -- says business
has picked up in the past few years.
While environmentalists aren't happy about potential hazards relating to mercury runoffs from the gold mines, the
town is using some of its newfound wealth to make improvements to the outdoors. Last year, the Convention and
Visitors Authority spent more than $400,000 improving the local trail system, including adding GPS navigation
capabilities and information kiosks. The authority has changed its slogan from "Explore Elko" to "Nevada With
Altitude." And in August, a new mountain biking trail is slated to open.

--- Page 106 ---
Page 106 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Other towns in Nevada are also looking beyond gaming, boosted by the Nevada Commission on Tourism. Four
years ago, the tourism body started a campaign to promote adventure travel in rural Nevada. Projects include
upgrading a 20-mile stretch along the Truckee River -- boulders are being added to make the water more
challenging for rafting. Authorities hope this will pique interest in towns along the route. "The more reasons we
give you to come to a town the better," says Bruce Bommarito, the commission's director.
Some communities are more specific in their focus. Mesquite, 80 miles northeast of Las Vegas, is positioning
itself as one of the state's prime destinations for golfers. Along with six luxury courses currently in the area,
including the challenging Wolf Creek, the city's economic planning committee says there are plans to add a new
course by fall of 2007.
Carson City, the state capital, is also promoting its connections to golf: "Gaming is not a major draw here," says
Janet Jones, a group sales manager for the city's convention and visitors bureau. Along with a new $6 million
clubhouse at the Arnold Palmer-designed Dayton Valley course, features were recently added to the town's Web
site. Travelers can now book tee times and hotel rooms online.
Even Laughlin, a town along the Colardo River once known as a budget-friendly gambling alternative to Las
Vegas, is rolling out a series of special events to draw more visitors. They recently launched a Hispanic heritage
festival, Viva! Laughlin, a four-day event in October with salsa dancing and performances by Latino and Tejano
musicians. This November, they're also expanding the International Rally and Motorsports Festival to include live
music.
FOR ELKO, ties to the Basque community set it apart from many Nevada towns. Euskara, the old Basque
language, can still be heard spoken and the Star Hotel is home to local Basques. Its eatery on the ground floor
dishes up traditional family-style dinners such as stews and baked beans, as well as Picon punch -- a strong drink
made of brandy, Picon liqueur and grenadine.
Every January, about 8,000 visitors gather in Elko for the National Cowboy Poetry Gathering, which celebrates
arts in the rural west. "Rural culture gets overlooked a lot," says Meg Glaser, the artistic director of the Western
Folklife Center, which organizes the event. "But there's definitely still a ranch identity to Elko."

Stocks End Day Below the Flat Line, As Investors Sour on Mixed Profit News
By Peter A. McKay
653 words
27 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS EDGED lower after a so-so batch of corporate earnings reports set the day's tone.
The prior evening, online retailer Amazon.com reported second-quarter profit that fell short of analysts'
expectations. And Boeing, one of the 30 members of the Dow Jones Industrial Average, reported a $160 million
loss due to a costly legal settlement with the federal government.
Troubled General Motors provided its shareholders with some pleasant surprises amid its latest spurt of red ink:
The auto maker reported a net loss of $3.18 billion, or $5.62 a share, for the latest quarter, more than triple the
year-ago loss. But the latest shortfall was exacerbated by costs associated with the company's restructuring, and
Wall Street analysts instead chose to focus on a 12% rise in revenue and GM's better-than-expected profit
excluding one-time charges.
Treasury bonds rose, while the dollar broke a two-day winning streak against both the euro and yen.
After spending much of the session in positive territory, the DJIA suffered a last-minute round of selling. At the
closing bell the blue-chip index was off 1.20 points at 11102.51, but still up 3.6% on the year.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index shed 0.48 point, to end at 1268.40, up 1.6% on the year. Both those
indexes were off less than 0.1% on the day. The Nasdaq Composite Index fared a bit worse, losing 0.2%, or 3.44
points, to end at 2070.46, down 6.1% on the year.
The Federal Reserve released its "beige book," an update from the Fed's 12 member banks on the state of their
regional economies over the past six weeks. Six of the banks cited slower growth -- the sort of downbeat signal
that many investors have welcomed lately because it may prompt the central bank to stop raising its key
interest-rate targets.
Investors already are anxious about the next big economic news release: Friday's report of
gross-domestic-product growth in the second quarter. That broad measure of economic output -- a preliminary
figure that will be revised later -- is expected to show growth of 3.2% in the period, down from a robust 5.6% in
the first quarter.
"At this point, an economic moderation would be healthy for us," said Citigroup economist Steve Wieting. "You're
already seeing the market respond to that," including a sag in so-called cyclical stocks that tend to do well when
the economy is on an upswing.
A drawdown in U.S. gasoline inventories pushed crude-oil futures higher. Oil for September delivery rose 0.3%, or
19 cents, to $73.94 a barrel, up 21.1% this year, on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Gold futures rose for the second straight day to $621.60 per troy ounce, up $4.00. Gold is now up $198.20, or
nearly 47%, from this time last year and is up 20% for the year to date.
Outside the U.S., stocks were little changed in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S.
shares, rose 0.03%, or 0.06 point, to 208.66.
In U.S. market action:

Dollar Gains Amid Wait for Data To Weigh Possible Moves by Fed
Currency Trading
Dollar Gains Amid Wait for Data To Weigh Possible Moves by Fed
By Azam Ahmed
500 words
25 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar moved higher in a quiet session, after falling sharply last week on reduced expectations of an
interest-rate increase by the Federal Reserve.
The slight turnaround for the dollar came as investors took profits on last week's dollar decline and as investors
corrected the extent to which the dollar fell, analysts said.
With no fresh U.S. data published, the dollar spent the North American session consolidating its gains from
overnight. Analysts expect that data later in the week could have an impact on currency markets.
Late in New York, the euro was at $1.2628 from $1.2697 late Friday. The dollar was at 116.78 yen from 116.18
yen, while the euro was at 147.53 yen from 147.50 yen. The dollar changed hands at 1.2467 Swiss francs from
1.2364 francs, and sterling fetched $1.8507 from $1.8590.
Analysts are quick to point out that the dollar's recovery overnight doesn't reflect new fundamental developments.
"I would be cautious to read too much into [yesterday's] market movements, other than the fact that we've had a
correction to the weaker dollar story, which tends to reinforce a range type mentality," said Alan Ruskin, head of
international strategy at RBC Greenwich in Conn.
"My guess is that we're just correcting what was a real big move" down in the dollar, he added.
The correction comes ahead of a host of data this week in the U.S. Investors are awaiting June existing-home
sales today, the Fed's so-called beige book tomorrow, and June durable-goods orders and new-home sales
Thursday.
"It's really the housing data and getting a cue on the housing sector that is going to be pretty interesting," said Mr.
Ruskin.
More important, however, will be the release of U.S. second-quarter data on gross domestic product Friday,
which is expected to confirm a slowdown, albeit an orderly one, of the economy.
"GDP is the kind of number where historically, in the breakdown, its been quite meaningful for some guidance to
longer-term investors," said Mr. Ruskin.
He added that the market will be closely watching to see how close the figure comes to expectations, and said the
dollar will likely react if the figure is significantly on either side of the 3.2% estimates for the second quarter.
With the Fed suggesting it is close to the end of its tightening cycle, data will be a major market driver this week
ahead of the FOMC meeting on Aug. 8, said analysts.
Elsewhere, global equities mounted a recovery after a poor performance last week, gold came off from its highest
levels of the session and crude-oil futures topped $75 a barrel. Emerging-market currencies also performed well,
in particular the Mexican peso and the Brazilian real.
---
Isabelle Lindenmayer in New York contributed to this article.

Dow Industrials Jump 182 Points To 2-Week High --- Rally Spurred by Strong Profits, Takeovers, Rice Trip to Mideast, But Gains Could Be...
Monday's Markets
Dow Industrials Jump 182 Points To 2-Week High --- Rally Spurred by Strong Profits, Takeovers, Rice Trip
to Mideast, But Gains Could Be Temporary
By E.S. Browning
467 words
25 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average surged 182.67 points to a two-week high, fueled by optimism about
corporate profits, about corporate takeovers and even about the Middle East.
Some on Wall Street worried the gains could fade, as has happened after other recent rallies, but for a day at
least the buyers were in charge.
The Dow finished at 11051.05, up 1.68% on the day and 3.1% on the year. Drug maker Merck, one of the
industrial average's 30 component stocks, released strong quarterly results and an upbeat profit forecast.
Schering-Plough, another drug stock that isn't in the blue-chip average, also reported strong profits.
Investors applauded news of several mergers and acquisitions, including hospital chain HCA's plans to be bought
out by investors and Advanced Micro Devices' planned acquisition of a smaller computer-chip maker. Such
takeovers sometimes fuel stock gains because they suggest that industry experts consider stock prices enticing.
Some analysts thought Secretary of State Condoleezza Rice's Middle East trip helped by creating hopes for a
resolution to the conflict. Gold prices pulled back $6.90 to $612.70 a troy ounce, possibly indicating less worry.
But crude-oil futures continued to rise, gaining 62 cents to $75.05 a barrel.
Some traders worried the rally could be partly a technical rebound after two days of declines.
"We have seen a couple of big rallies like this in the past few weeks, and they have faded the very next day," said
Stephen Sachs, director of trading at mutual-fund group Rydex Investments in Rockville, Md. He said investors
will be watching whether the gains continue today.
Investors are nervous about the government's release of preliminary second-quarter economic-growth figures on
Friday. Those could indicate whether the economy is cooling enough for the Federal Reserve to stop raising
interest rates.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 1.66%, or 20.62 points, to 1260.91, also a two-week high. It is up 1%
for 2006. The volatile Nasdaq Composite Index jumped 2.05%, or 41.45 points, to 2061.84, still down 6.5% this
year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose and bonds fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 1/32, or 31 cents for each $1,000 invested, pushing
the yield up to 5.049%. The 30-year bond was down 3/32 to yield 5.105%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 116.78 yen, up from 116.18 yen, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2628 from $1.2697.

MarketWatch Weekend Investor: Cash Is King Now As Opportunities Are Harder to Find
MarketWatch Weekend Investor: Cash Is King Now As Opportunities Are Harder to Find
By Herb Greenberg
800 words
22 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
CALL ME boring, but I like cash -- especially now. Even with the risks of inflation, which erodes the value of a
dollar over time, cash is hard to ignore when you get paid 5% (and rising) for doing absolutely nothing other than
trying to pick between nearly riskless Treasurys, CDs or good old-fashioned money-market accounts.
My fully funded 401(k) and a large chunk of my IRA are in cash. Heck, I actually invest in a wide range of CDs
(yes, I like watching paint dry), and watching short-term interest rates rise -- which helps cash -- has become a
favorite new hobby.
That isn't to say stocks have turned poisonous. For stocks, workplace rules limit me to mutual funds, and I have
woefully too little in Dodge &Cox Balanced Fund, acquired the day before the market low in 2002; with dividends,
it's up 67%. (OK, I thought the market would fall further and better prices would emerge.)
Also in the portfolio: a ridiculously small amount of Olstein Financial Alert Fund, bought a month or two after the
market low in 2002. As stock prices were starting to roll over in May, the portfolio added Leuthold Grizzly Short
Fund, which shorts stocks. (It's doing quite nicely, thank you, as a small hedge against everything else.) At the
same time, as gold prices were falling a few weeks ago, I loaded up on iShares Comex Gold Trust, making up for
the missed opportunity of not buying it sooner. Both of those funds replaced Matthews Asian Growth &Income
Fund and Allianz RCM Biotechnology Fund, which had gained 100% and more than 50%, respectively, since
being added to my portfolio.
While I'm a reporter who focuses on companies, not markets, cash is currently king in my world. I'm always on the
hunt for opportunities, but that hunt is getting harder for many investors. Robert Rodriguez, chief executive of First
Pacific Advisors and manager of FPA Capital Fund, is 43% in Treasurys -- soon to be 48% after he rakes in the
profits from his stake in Michaels Stores, which is in the process of going private. He simply can't find enough
stocks that meet his buying criteria, and he doesn't believe the S&P 500-stock index can earn more than 5% a
year for the foreseeable future. Even if it earns 7% a year, he says, "a 2% incremental risk return is not sufficient
compensation for investment risk."
He adds, "You never know the value of liquidity until you need it and don't have it. We're thinking about changing
the name of the fund to the Tylenol Fund, because when others get an extreme headache and need relief from
the pain, we will be more than happy to provide them pain relief. But to get that Tylenol pill, you will have to pay a
high price in the form of decreased valuation of securities you want to sell."
Not that Mr. Rodriguez has always been that negative. And, with having as little as 3% cash at one point, he
certainly doesn't believe cash is always better than stocks. As Bank of America Securities Chief Investment
Strategist Tom McManus reminds me, "I don't think one can make a long-term case to be in cash, especially
relative to stocks over the past 20-25 years," a period during which large and small cap stocks returned, on
average, more than 10% a year. You would have to "cherry pick" time periods, in which cash was the better
choice, he says.
To be sure, with the exception of an 18-month period in 2000 and 2001, and a portion of the 1970s, cash has
largely been trash. But based on current yields, assuming the market continues to wallow, cash is on track to beat
stocks this year.
"This whole geopolitical thing hits the market at its most vulnerable time, as there is an inflection point in
corporate growth and profitability," says Doug Kass of Seabreeze Partners hedge fund. "So, with the risk-free rate
of return going up, cash seems so obvious."

--- Page 115 ---
Page 115 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Which, of course, is why the concept of cash is so hard for some people to grasp. Sometimes things that are the
most obvious are the hardest to see. With cash, the most obvious is that at the end of the day, no matter what
happens in times of uncertainty, you still have it.
---
Herb Greenberg is a senior columnist at MarketWatch, a financial-news Web site owned by Dow Jones, publisher
of The Wall Street Journal. He doesn't own stocks (except his employer's), nor sell individual stocks short or
invest in hedge funds.

The Fed's Logic
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Fed's Logic
699 words
20 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Every investor likes to believe that the bad news is behind him, so stocks understandably soared yesterday on
Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke's allegedly dovish testimony to Congress on the future course of interest rates. For
our money, however, yesterday's bigger and less reassuring news was the Commerce Department's June
inflation report.
The June consumer price index proved, if more proof were needed, just how big a monetary policy mistake the
Fed made from late 2003 through 2005. The government's price indices operate on a lag, often as long as 24
months, and they are now showing a marked inflation revival that too many economists and pundits, including
more than a few Fed Governors, have been reluctant to acknowledge.
Overall prices rose in June by 0.2%, a deceptively low number since it included a big decline in energy prices for
the month; the real signal is that inflation has set in across the breadth of the U.S. economy. For the last 12
months prices have climbed at an annual rate of 4.3%, and for the last three months by 5.1%. So-called "core"
inflation -- sans food and energy -- rose 0.3% in June and is now well above the 2% annual rate that the Fed says
is at the top of its tolerable range. For the last three months, "core" inflation is up a scary 3.6%.
So when Mr. Bernanke declared in his testimony yesterday that "Inflation has been higher than we had
anticipated in February," he was understating things by a good measure. The Fed has been wrong for a lot longer
than February; it has merely taken the conventional price indices this long to make some people believe it.
One striking, and perhaps worrying, part of Mr. Bernanke's testimony yesterday was his implicit logic that real
economic growth will soon slow down, therefore we don't have to worry about inflation as much, ergo interest
rates may not have to keep rising. Mr. Bernanke is too good an economist to actually believe this. Real GDP
growth (defined as growth less inflation) can decline even as prices are rising fast and nominal growth continues
to roar. What the Fed should be watching is less real GDP than real prices, and this is where the Fed took its eye
off the ball by staying too loose for too long.
Mr. Bernanke is still scrambling to catch up, and the danger looking ahead is that the Fed will have to raise rates
more than if it had tightened money faster and earlier in the final years of the Alan Greenspan era. Investors in
stocks were betting yesterday that the interest-rate increases are almost over, though we wouldn't bet our own
mortgage on it. Stocks were also perhaps oversold last week due to Middle East worries. More price increases
are baked in the cake in coming months, and those will surely test whether the Fed can make its
much-ballyhooed "pause" in raising rates at its August meeting -- and for how long.
Our own belief is that the Fed has substantially increased the chances of a recession in the next year or two by
failing to blunt inflationary expectations early enough. Let's hope the economy's current supply-side momentum,
led by very strong business investment, and stronger global growth will offset the impact of higher rates and $75
oil. Monday's Fed report of a big 0.8% jump in June industrial production was an encouraging sign that business
spending and manufacturing remain strong.
One question for the Fed, and especially for its staff, is whether its inflation mistake will lead to any policy
introspection. Mr. Bernanke is surely right, as he said yesterday, that "monetary policy makers operate in an
environment of uncertainty." But those policy makers also continue to downplay, or even dismiss, the relevance of
such real-time inflation signals as a weak dollar and the soaring price of commodities including gold. Those prices
have been warning the Fed about future inflation for a long time. Some policy reflection can be good for the soul,
and even better for the economy.

A Penny Unsaved
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
A Penny Unsaved
437 words
20 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
For anyone who has ever reached down to pick up a stray penny, we've got bad news: There's a move afoot in
Congress to abolish the coin with Honest Abe's likeness. It turns out that, thanks to rising prices for zinc and
copper, every penny now costs slightly more than one cent for the U.S. Mint to produce. (Nickels may be in
danger too: They now cost about 5.5 cents to produce.)
The decline of the penny as a storehouse of value is a depressing lesson of the corrosive impact of the thief of
inflation over time. A penny now has one-eighth the purchasing power it did in 1950 -- and then the coin was 95%
copper, versus 3% today. The penny's devaluation may explain why the slogan on the face of the penny reads "In
God We Trust," not "In Government We Trust."
The penny is today regarded by many Americans as an irrelevance and a pocket nuisance. That's one reason
Congressman Jim Kolbe of Arizona is sponsoring a bill that would round all purchases to the nearest nickel, thus
making the penny functionally obsolete. Many retail businesses have already taken this step, charging final prices
that are rounded to the nearest nickel, dime or quarter. In terms of reducing transaction costs and the hassles and
expense of holding money, that practice often makes sense.
Not to be melodramatic, but eliminating the penny would wave a symbolic white flag before the forces of inflation.
Taking coins out of circulation is an act that one associates with nations like Argentina, Bolivia and Mexico that
periodically degrade their peso currencies and create hyperinflation. Moreover, the market works. If American
consumers don't find value in using pennies, they are free to conduct their transactions without them -- or drop the
change in the jar for Jerry's Kids. It turns out that one social value of pennies is that they help charities collect
millions of dollars in donations each year -- yes, one red cent at a time.
We're believers in hard currencies that retain value, and we would note that the economy turned on, well, a penny
in the early 1980s when Ronald Reagan pledged to sweat out the double-digit inflation of the 1970s and make the
dollar "as good as gold again." What kind of signal does it send if Congress announces that its currency is not
even as good as zinc and copper?
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: A Thought for Your Penny -- It's Not Worth a Cent" -- WSJ Aug. 31,
2006)

Bernanke Sends Stocks Skyward --- Industrials Jump 212.19 Points On Hint at Pause in Rate Rises; Yahoo Tumbles a Record 22%
Wednesday's Markets
Bernanke Sends Stocks Skyward --- Industrials Jump 212.19 Points On Hint at Pause in Rate Rises;
Yahoo Tumbles a Record 22%
By E.S. Browning
1,049 words
20 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average soared, recording the year's second-strongest gain, after Federal Reserve
Chairman Ben Bernanke offered hope for an end to interest-rate increases. Treasury-bond prices also surged.
In testimony to the Senate Banking Committee, Mr. Bernanke didn't rule out another boost to the Fed's
overnight-lending rate target when Fed policy makers meet again Aug. 8. But he said he sees signs of moderating
economic growth, and that inflation should cool down in response.
The Fed has been raising rates for the past two years in an effort to prevent inflation, and investors have been
worried that higher rates could stifle business profits and make other investments, such as money-market funds,
attractive alternatives to stocks.
After slumping below 11000 last week as fighting raged in the Gaza Strip and in Lebanon, the Dow Jones
Industrial Average yesterday pushed back above that milestone, jumping 212.19 points, or 1.96%, to 11011.42. It
was the sharpest point or percentage gain since June 29, and left the Dow industrials ahead 2.7% for 2006. Other
indexes also rose sharply.
"People were on edge that Mr. Bernanke might say something they weren't expecting," said stock trader Michael
Driscoll at New York brokerage firm Bear Stearns. After the reassuring comments from the Fed chief, "you could
see the market spike up," he said. Trading volume was heavy.
But he and other market analysts warned the market could face more headwinds this summer. They said
yesterday's rally was partly just a technical rebound from excessive selling the week before.
What's more, Mr. Bernanke made no promises about how soon he would stop raising rates, which he indicated
will depend on economic data. He suggested he doesn't want to raise rates too high, for fear of killing the
economic recovery. But he noted there is a risk that inflation could remain too high even as the economy slows. In
that case, the Fed might be forced to keep raising rates, even if doing so damages the economy.
"There is a possibility that Mr. Bernanke is being too optimistic" about how soon he will be able to stop raising
rates, said Gordon Fowler, chief investment officer at Philadelphia money-management firm Glenmede Trust.
Inflation has a way of hanging around longer than people expect, said Mr. Fowler, who worries that the market
could remain choppy as the summer wears on.
"Until the stock market sees some real turnaround (in the inflation outlook), it may go through spasms of
happiness on some days, and on other days it can become more concerned."
Shortly before Mr. Bernanke spoke, the Labor Department released data indicating that core consumer-price
inflation, which doesn't count volatile food and energy prices, was stronger than expected in June, remaining
above the level Mr. Bernanke has indicated he considers acceptable.
Wall Street shrugged off those kinds of concerns, at least for a day, and focused instead on the hope that inflation
would slow.
Bond prices jumped higher on Mr. Bernanke's testimony, pushing bond yields lower as bond investors, too,
looked ahead to an end to Fed rate increases.

--- Page 124 ---
Page 124 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Another reason for Wall Street's optimism is that investors received strong quarterly profit news from the likes of
International Business Machines and J.P. Morgan Chase. And a federal judge overturned a Maryland law
requiring Wal-Mart Stores to make bigger contributions to employee health care.
What's more, crude-oil futures fell for a third consecutive day after hitting a 23-year high of $77.03 Friday. Crude
futures dropped 88 cents to $72.66 a barrel. On top of hopes for containment of the Middle East violence, a
government report showed U.S. oil inventories surprisingly high last week and gasoline demand lower than
expected.
Some of the day's gains were artificial, however. Some investors who were betting on stock declines by borrowing
stock and selling it decided to take profits. To do that, they had to buy the stock back and return it to lenders, and
their purchases helped support stock prices.
After the closing bell, investors got quarterly profit news from companies including Intel, eBay, Apple and
Motorola that could affect trading today. Apple and Motorola reported strong product shipments and eBay
forecast higher full-year profits, and their stocks rose 8.5% and 5.7%, respectively, in after-hours trading. Intel's
quarterly profit exceeded investor expectations, but the chip-maker's sales forecast was weaker than expected.
Its stock slipped 0.59% after hours.
Yahoo made history by tumbling 22% in regular-hours Nasdaq trading, the Internet-search company's biggest
one-day percentage decline ever -- and a $10 billion blow to its market capitalization. The stock's close at $25.20
is the lowest in more than two years.
Also in regular hours, the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 1.86%, or 22.95 points, to 1259.81. It was the
big stock index's third-largest gain of the year in point and percentage terms, leaving it ahead 0.9% for 2006. The
Nasdaq Composite Index, full of technology stocks, rose 1.83%, or 37.49 points, to 2080.71, still down 5.7% this
year.
Gold futures halted a two-day slide, surging $13.30 to $641.60 a troy ounce.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.81%, or 3.62 points, to 203.94.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.8 billion shares,
as 2,886 stocks rose and 471 fell.
Bond prices gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 19/32, or $5.94 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
down to 5.061%. The 30-year bond was up 28/32 to yield 5.106%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, the currency traded at 116.84 yen, down from 117.38, while the euro
rose against the dollar to $1.2600 from $1.2508.

Oil Closes at Low for Month Amid Positive Report on Gas --- Data Ease Supply Worries, Leading to Close of $72.66; Gold Increases Sharply
Commodities Report
Oil Closes at Low for Month Amid Positive Report on Gas --- Data Ease Supply Worries, Leading to Close
of $72.66; Gold Increases Sharply
By Matt Chambers
536 words
20 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude-oil prices closed at their lowest level for the month, hit by reduced concern about escalating conflict in the
Middle East and after government data showed U.S. gasoline stockpiles unexpectedly rose.
The front-month August crude contract on the New York Mercantile Exchange closed down 88 cents at $72.66 a
barrel, helped by a late rally after prices dropped as low as $71.65. It was crude's lowest closing price since June
29. September Brent traded on the IntercontinentalExchange was down 46 cents at $73.90 a barrel.
Crude prices have fallen 7.3% since hitting a record $78.40 a barrel Friday on concerns that fighting between
Israel and Hezbollah would escalate to include Iran and threaten Persian Gulf oil supplies.
"It doesn't look like we'll see Iran or Syria come into the conflict in the near term," said Mike Fitzpatrick, a broker
and analyst in New York at Fimat brokerage, noting that this was forcing some with long positions in oil to sell. Mr.
Fitzpatrick added that he expects prices to rebound.
"I think it's a buying opportunity, the situation in the Middle East hasn't really changed," he said, noting that global
demand was supported by strong growth figures from China this week.
Helping the selloff was a U.S. Energy Information Administration report showing gasoline stockpiles unexpectedly
grew last week and topped their year-earlier level for the first time since March.
Analysts surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had been expecting a 1.1 million-barrel fall in stocks, well below the
1.5 million barrel rise reported in the weekly data.
Analysts said the market focused on the situation in the Middle East and the surprise gain in gasoline inventories
rather than comments from Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke.
Fighting continued between Israel and Hezbollah forces, with firefights erupting along Lebanon's southwestern
border with Israel. Concerns that the conflict would escalate into a regional war and see Iran blocking Persian
Gulf oil exports have eased, with Israel saying it doesn't want to widen the attack to include Hezbollah supporters
Syria or Iran.
Katherine Spector, an analyst at J.P. Morgan in New York, said the recent pullback in prices hasn't changed her
bullish attitude toward oil prices. "I don't think we are done with this rally," Ms. Spector said.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose sharply in the wake of
comments from Fed Chairman Bernanke that generated optimism that a pause in monetary tightening may not be
far away. His remarks caused the U.S. dollar to reverse some of its recent gains, which provided a catalyst to buy
gold. Nearby July gold closed up $13.30 to $641.60 an ounce.
NATURAL GAS: Futures rebounded after two straight days of losses, settling up for the first time this week.
Benchmark August futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange settled up 30.7 cents at $5.862 per million
British thermal units.

Hanging Loose
Hanging Loose
By David Malpass
1,336 words
20 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The current turmoil in world financial markets reflects geopolitical tensions as well as a U.S. inflation problem. But
concerns about Japan's interest-rate hike contributing to a "global monetary tightening" are off target.
On July 14, Japan increased its interest rate to 0.25%. This began what will almost surely be a protracted
rate-hiking process, as Japan's economy and rate structure return to a more healthy equilibrium after the
traumatic 1991-2004 deflation spiral. Even with rising rates, Japan's monetary policy is likely to remain easy for
many quarters, paralleling the U.S. post-deflation monetary accommodation of 2004 and 2005.
Several indicators reveal Japan's monetary policy as downright loose. The 0.25% annualized overnight interest
rate is lower than inflation (0.6% year-over-year), leaving real interest rates negative, a clear sign of easy
monetary policy. The interest rate is also well below the 3% expected 2006 nominal GDP growth rate, making
borrowing profitable -- even if it is to excess. With the yen price of gold 90% above its 10-year average, the Bank
of Japan is providing its strongest monetary accommodation since the 1970s.
Each of these indicators is an about-face from Japan's previous monetary stance, confirming that the country has
successfully reversed the deflationary monetary policy of the 1990s. That policy featured high real interest rates,
nominal interest rates above the nominal growth rate, and a superstrong yen contributing to deflation. With the
monetary-policy reversal, growth is running 3% in real and nominal terms despite a shrinking labor force, and
unemployment fell to 4% in May, the lowest since 1998.
Since inflation lags monetary policy, the risk for Japan over the next two years -- like the U.S. Fed's anti-deflation
bias in 2004 and 2005 -- is that it will provide too much stimulus. If so, this will distort Japan's economy toward
real estate and excess liquidity, as well as create an inflation problem down the line.
In its July 14 statement, the Bank of Japan sounded like the Fed when it started hiking rates above 1% in June
2004. The BoJ said it "will adjust the level of the policy interest rate gradually in light of developments in economic
activity and prices . . ." This is akin to the "measured" hikes the Fed promised. The BoJ also stated that: "An
accommodative monetary environment ensuing from very low interest rates will probably be maintained for some
time." The Fed used the phrase "policy accommodation can be maintained for a considerable period" in 2003,
and explicitly pursued an "accommodative" monetary policy through November 2005. The BoJ added that it will
"conduct monetary policy by carefully assessing economic activity and prices." This sounds like "data
dependence," the Fed's current keep-the-options-open stance.
Some analysts are calling Japan's move a tightening of monetary policy, feeding their concerns about a global
slowdown. Rather than look at the level of real rates, they point to the increase in Japan's nominal rates -- a hike
is a hike, three steps and a stumble. This theory -- that rate hikes slow an economy regardless of their level --
contributed to the incorrect 2004, 2005 and 2006 forecasts of a U.S. slowdown. Even after 17 rate hikes, the U.S.
continues to turn in very strong growth. U.S. industrial production rose at a 6.8% annual rate in the second
quarter, up from a 5% rate in the first quarter and the fastest quarterly growth since 1999. Real GDP growth may
reach a 3.5% rate in the second quarter on top of 5.6% growth in the first quarter, the strongest two-quarter
growth since 2003.
Thus, rather than cause a slowdown, Japan's experience with rate hikes from a low level may parallel the U.S.
experience. As U.S. rates rose beginning in 2004, growth was stronger than expected, distortions from excess
liquidity more dramatic, and inflation more pronounced: U.S. CPI prices rose 4.7% from October 2004 through
September 2005 and are still rising at a 4% rate.

--- Page 128 ---
Page 128 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Like the U.S., Japan's household sector is a big net creditor, while the corporate sector has deleveraged, arguing
that its economy will also be relatively insulated from even a long series of rate hikes. Japan's profit growth is
strong, but so are memories of deflation, causing a culture of corporate caution. Like those in the U.S., Japan's
big corporations are hiring and investing sparingly and keeping inventories at rock bottom, providing room for
smaller, cash-hungry businesses.
Along with rate hikes, strict monetarists are citing Japan's shrinking monetary base as a concern in the Japanese
and global growth outlook. Early in 2006, the BoJ announced and then carried out a reduction in bank reserves. It
sounds ominous. The monetary base (the sum of yen currency plus commercial bank reserves on deposit at the
central bank) fell from $969 billion in January to $807 billion in June. But simultaneously, the turnover of yen is
speeding up. In 2005, each high-powered yen was used, on average, only 4.5 times, versus 16.2 times for each
dollar. The turnover of yen jumped to 5.5 times in the second quarter of 2006 and is probably headed much
higher as the cost of holding idle yen rises with interest rates.
To double-check the accommodative signal from Japan's rising monetary velocity, the level of Japan's monetary
base is large even after the recent downsizing. Japan's $807 billion June monetary base is as big as the $805
billion U.S. monetary base, which powers an economy nearly three times as big as Japan's.
The increase in the velocity of Japan's money supply is likely to overpower the reduction in its size. With the BoJ's
big balance sheet sitting idle, monetary base peaked at $1.07 trillion in January 2005, with the BoJ 40% bigger
than the Fed. With the former's interest rate at 0%, commercial banks lost nothing by maintaining extra bank
reserves. At the prospect of rising interest rates -- and despite shrinking reserves -- bank lending is growing for
the first time in a decade.
Looking beyond Japan, nominal interest rates are rising in many countries, broadening the economic debate from
a supposed U.S.-and-Japan slowdown to a global one. Arguing against this view, real interest rates are still
generally low and monetary bases large relative to GDP. And despite a string of rate hikes around the world, real
global growth is enjoying its strongest four-year run since the early 1970s. China showed 11.3% second quarter
growth, spurred by a stable currency, rural development, rural tax cuts and price liberalization. For the first time,
China's rural incomes outgrew urban incomes, providing an important stabilizer.
Western Europe's growth, typically less than half the U.S. rate due to high unemployment and labor inflexibility,
should exceed 2% in 2006. After a much-remarked pause in 2005, the U.K.'s house prices are rising again and
inflation jumped to 2.5% in June. Rather than reflecting a global monetary tightening, strong growth, high
commodity prices and rising inflation in the U.S., China, Japan and Europe indicate accommodation.
For Japan, the lessons from the U.S. reflation experience are clear. The level of real interest rates is a better
indicator than the change in nominal rates. Core inflation has a multiyear lag from monetary policy. Deflation
ended cleanly when the value of the currency exited deflationary territory (which the U.S. accomplished in 2003
and Japan in 2004).
The U.S. will likely shift to an inflation-fighting stance in the next few months, giving Japan the chance to assess
the difficulty of stopping an inflation bulge after a deflation. How the U.S. handles this challenge will provide vital
economic insight for Japan's monetary policy.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief economist at Bear Stearns.

Dollar Strengthens Against Yen, Euro After Solid Data
By Azam Ahmed
544 words
19 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar advanced ahead of today's U.S. consumer-inflation data and testimony by the head of the Federal
Reserve.
Solid U.S. data yesterday helped to push the dollar higher, with the euro falling under $1.25 and the dollar rising
to about 117.50 yen during the day. Some analysts attributed part of the move to technical trading, as automatic
orders to buy dollars were triggered when the U.S. currency passed key levels.
With the dollar at its strongest versus the yen in nearly three months, and the euro near its lowest levels since late
April, some analysts argued that there is now more downside potential for the dollar than for further gains.
In late New York trading, the euro was at $1.2508, down from $1.2523 late Monday. The dollar gained to 117.38
yen from 117.19 yen and to 1.2522 Swiss francs from 1.2480 francs. However, sterling rose to $1.8269 from
$1.8189.
Immediately after the publication of the U.S. producer-price index for June and Treasury Income Capital system
data for May, the dollar seemed to weaken slightly. But as investors reconsidered the uptick in wholesale inflation
and the higher-than-expected net inflows from foreign investors into the U.S., the dollar received a belated boost.
For yesterday and likely today, "you have a period in which our focus will switch back to the inflation story in the
U.S.," said David Durrant, chief strategist at Julius Baer Investment Management in New York.
After the higher PPI number and with the consumer-price index and Chairman Bernanke's comments scheduled
for today, the move in the dollar showed that "in the very short term, there's a bias towards a stronger U.S. dollar
on the back of higher U.S. rates," said Mr. Durrant.
The market is currently pricing in roughly a 65% chance for a Fed interest-rate increase in August, up from about
50% late Monday.
Mr. Durrant expects Mr. Bernanke to signal that the Fed will continue to reinforce that the central bank is
"continuing on a data-dependent stream," meaning that "the probability of another rate hike remains very real."
CPI, a figure the Fed watches closely, is expected to gain 0.2% for June, after a 0.4% rise in May. If the core
number is higher than expected, the dollar will most likely rally, according to some analysts. But others note that
the dollar seems to be near the top of its recent range, following a surprising recovery as risk aversion has
pushed up oil and gold prices and investors adjust their currency positions.
Unrest in the Middle East has convinced investors to balance dollar and euro positions, which has seen the dollar
surge against its rivals. Mark Chandler, currency strategist at Brown Brothers Harriman in New York, notes that
recent economic reports suggest growth is moderating and inflation signals have dimmed. He predicts Mr.
Bernanke will "steer the boat down the middle" while still leaving the door open for a pause in interest-rate
increases. That, in turn, would likely cause the dollar to fall.

Common Sense: Gloom Over Summer of Woe Appears Overdone
Personal Finance
Common Sense: Gloom Over Summer of Woe Appears Overdone
By James B. Stewart SmartMoney
703 words
19 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE WORLD suddenly seems beset with calamities, with missiles flying across the Israel-Lebanon border,
militia-led killing sprees in Baghdad and terrorist bombings in India.
You can hardly blame people for being on edge, or for losing their appetite for stocks. Slumping market averages
and oil prices at a record high added to the pervasive gloom.
Investing in stocks is an inherently optimistic step. People who are by nature optimistic tend to have a higher
tolerance for risk, and thus a greater appetite for stocks, the riskiest but highest-yielding asset class over time.
Pessimists gravitate towards inflation-adjusted Treasury bonds, gold and, of course, cash. These were among the
favored havens last week, when pessimism was rampant.
Blind optimism can be as damaging to superior investment results as rampant pessimism. Last week's woes,
many of them related to deeper, seemingly intractable problems, are indeed vexing and significant, calling for
thoughtful leadership. Curiously, a few months ago, when stock prices were at record levels, investors were
shrugging off the world's problems. Now the pendulum has swung to the other extreme, and investors seem
unduly preoccupied by them.
The fact is that the world economy is so diversified and dynamic that most tragedies have little or no long-term
effect. I don't mean to sound indifferent to human suffering, and I readily acknowledge that the health of an
economy or the state of the markets are hardly the only measure of global well-being, or even the most important.
But this is an investment column.
Many horrible things have happened over the years. Whatever the psychological effect or impact on history, they
didn't stop economic growth -- or rising stock markets -- as much as the Great Depression, which caused a global
economic contraction.
It isn't hard to imagine global catastrophes that might indeed have an equivalent devastating effect. The news is
filled with dire warnings about everything from global warming to an Avian flu pandemic. But nothing that
happened last week even comes close.
My sense is that markets usually shrug off such events, but sometimes they overreact, creating buying
opportunities. I've already done my buying until the market hits my next threshold, which is 1900 on the Nasdaq
Composite. But if you haven't taken advantage of the recent correction and have cash to deploy, this remains a
buying opportunity.
Last week also brought with it a smattering of profit warnings as the quarterly earnings season gets under way.
So far, I see nothing earth-shaking here, as this time of year always brings warnings. If anything, given the long
duration of this economic cycle and analysts' high expectations for profit growth, I'm surprised there haven't been
more warnings.
Analysts tend to see broad trends in these warnings, but often they're company-specific. It will be interesting to
see if 3M's warning really signals widespread woe in the manufacturing sector and trouble in the LCD-television
market, or if EMC's reduced expectations are typical of the beaten-down technology sector.
Two companies to watch will be Corning, which also makes flat-panel screens, and Network Appliance, which
competes with EMC in data storage. Corning is scheduled to release results July 25, and Network Appliance on

--- Page 131 ---
Page 131 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Aug. 16. I own both companies, and so far neither has issued a warning. In fact, Network Appliance affirmed
earnings guidance early yesterday.
Still, that hasn't stopped investors from fleeing the stocks over the past few days. At a recent price of about $22,
Corning is 27% off its 52-week high of just under $30 in April (just after I recommended selling shares or calls). At
around $27, Network Appliance is about 30% off its high for the year.
If you're shopping for bargains during this summer of widespread woe, these may be two stocks to consider.
---

personal investing strategy. Unlike Dow Jones reporters, he may have positions in the stocks he writes about. For
his past columns, see: www.smartmoney.com/wsjcommon .

Proposed ETFs Offer Niche Investments
By Ian Salisbury
221 words
19 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
D2
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Several products on file with the Securities and Exchange Commission propose to offer investors
access to ever-smaller corners of the stock market through narrowly focused ETFs.
ETFs, or exchange-traded funds, resemble index-based mutual funds but trade on an exchange like a stock.
Among the offerings awaiting the regulatory green light are a family of funds by a new ETF player that aims to
divide the health-care industry into subsectors almost akin to branches of medicine, and funds that slice
commodities into sectors. The family of 19 funds from Ferghana-Wellspring LLC, to be known as HealthShares,
would let investors choose just companies involved in diagnostics, ophthalmology, infectious disease or
cardiology, among others.
Deutsche Bank AG recently filed for seven new commodity funds tracking energy, oil, precious metals, gold,
silver, base metals and agriculture. The offerings will build on the DB Commodity Index Tracking Fund, which
tracks a basket of commodities and has collected about $600 million in assets since it was launched in February.
The firm has also filed to offer an ETF that would invest in a portfolio of currencies, adjusting bets to reflect
interest rates.
The market already boasts individual exchange-traded funds that track oil and silver and two that track gold.

1.71, or 0.14%, to 1234.49, down four sessions in a row.
There was pretty much a stalemate between bulls and bears because "there were a lot of cross currents today,"
said Ed Keon, chief investment strategist at Prudential Equity Group. "In the Middle East, there was some news
suggesting a cease fire, but the violence continued. There were also mixed earnings, and the June U.S. industrial
production report could be read as either positive or negative depending on which part you focused on. In other
words, the bulls and bears both got facts to support their case today."
Citigroup fell $1.18, or 2.5%, to $46.40, the worst performer on the Dow industrial average. Profit edged up 4% in
the second quarter, as the world's largest financial-services company was propelled by strong investment banking
results, but suffered from rising expenses and sluggishness in its consumer business.

monetary-policy testimony tomorrow to the Senate Banking Committee. Investors hope for an indication whether
Mr. Bernanke is thinking of raising interest rates further at the Fed's next policy meeting, Aug. 8.

summary of the minutes of the Fed's last meeting, in June, which also should offer insight into the Fed's mood.

Keeping Cool Amid Global Strife --- Past Crises in Middle East Suggest Stock Sellers May Be Overreacting; A Fear of Oil-Market Disruptions
Abreast of the Market
Keeping Cool Amid Global Strife --- Past Crises in Middle East Suggest Stock Sellers May Be
Overreacting; A Fear of Oil-Market Disruptions
By E.S. Browning
1,086 words
17 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AS STOCKS have crumbled following the sudden outbreak of Middle East fighting, money managers have
reminded themselves of the maxim British banker Nathan Mayer Rothschild is said to have uttered during the
Napoleonic wars: Buy to the sound of cannons, sell to the sound of trumpets.
A look at past Middle East blowups suggests the founder of the British branch of the legendary banking family
had a point. Almost never has Middle East fighting done long-term damage to the U.S. stock market. Short-term
stock pullbacks typically have been opportunities to buy at a reduced price. Only when economic fundamentals
have been seriously damaged, usually in the form of a disruption in oil supplies, has Middle East fighting led to
serious market trouble.
"Experience would suggest that the market tends to overreact to these crises," says Robert Morris, chief
investment officer at Jersey City, N.J., fund-management group Lord Abbett. "If it turns out that the worst case [an
oil disruption] doesn't occur, this is going to look like a terrific opportunity in the rearview mirror."
The problem, of course, is that there never is a guarantee that the economic fundamentals will be fine. Stocks
weathered the 1967 Six Day War well, and they also did well following the two U.S. invasions of Iraq, according to
data compiled by Ned Davis Research. But they ran into trouble after the 1973 October war. Why? Probably
because a few days after that war began, Arab oil exporters declared an embargo on oil shipments to countries
that had supported Israel, and quadrupled export prices.
Stocks also fell in the wake of Iran-Iraq fighting that began in 1980, but that decline probably had more to do with
the recession that hit the U.S. around that time. The Federal Reserve was sending interest rates to nosebleed
levels to rein in the inflation that had been caused, in part, by soaring oil prices. A couple years later, U.S. stocks
entered a prolonged bull market.
What investors have feared for several years now, as oil prices have risen steadily, is that a Middle East crisis
could disrupt oil markets again, at a time when demand is heavy and supplies are tight. Little wonder that
crude-oil futures finished Friday at $77.03 a barrel, the highest price since oil started trading on the New York
Mercantile Exchange in 1983. Crude still is well below the inflation-adjusted high hit in 1980 -- $98.58 in 2006
dollars -- but it has more than quadrupled from the $17.45 at which it traded in 2001. Increasingly, energy costs
are a tax on businesses and consumers alike, holding back growth.
Gold, to which some investors flee in times of trouble, rose $13.70, or 2.1%, to $666.60 a troy ounce on Friday. It
is now $53.20 off the 26-year high hit May 11, but up 29% for 2006. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell to
10739.35 points, its lowest level in a month. It is down more than 900 points, almost 8%, since hitting a six-year
high of 11642.65 on May 10. The volatile Nasdaq Composite Index, at 2037.35, is at its lowest level in more than
a year.
One fear is that Iran, sponsor of the Hezbollah militias fighting Israel, will get drawn into the conflict and cut back
on oil shipments as a result. Markets already had been disrupted this year by concerns that tensions between
Western countries and Iran over that country's nuclear program could affect Iranian oil supplies.
"In the financial markets we have witnessed the expected reaction: global selling of stocks, a rising gold price and
a flight to the U.S. dollar and into U.S. Treasury bonds. This is typical in times like these," wrote David Kotok,
president of New Jersey money-management firm Cumberland Advisors in a note to clients Friday about the
current Middle East fighting.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Kotok added that his investment accounts already were holding as much as 25% or 30% of their value in
cash, having taken a more defensive position during the market turmoil earlier this year. Once he sees signs that
the risk of a wider war has diminished, he said, he will start buying stocks that have been knocked down by the
worries. But he added that he will be looking at other factors as well -- the housing market, consumer spending,
oil prices and global interest rates. Investors also will be looking closely at corporate profit reports due in large
volume this week and next.
If the war doesn't spill over into Iran and if oil prices cool, investors will revert to the kinds of worries that had been
making stocks gyrate since May: whether rising global interest rates will stifle corporate-profit growth and cut back
on the availability of cash for people to invest.
On that score, they will have plenty to worry about this week. Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke testifies
Wednesday to the Senate Banking Committee on interest-rate policy, the same day that June consumer-price
and housing-starts data are released. Thursday, the Federal Reserve releases a summary of the minutes of its
June meeting. That will offer investors a closer look at whether policy makers share the market's hopes for a
pause in rate increases at the Fed's August or September meeting.
"I am expecting a slowdown in consumer spending here, but nothing that leads to recession," says Bank of
America market strategist Joseph Quinlan. "I think the fundamentals are supportive of a market rally into the
fourth quarter, assuming these geopolitical issues settle down and oil pulls back.
"But if oil goes too high, too quickly because of these geopolitical risks, you have to recalculate just how retail
sales will be affected. The airlines will be affected. So will the industrial sector. It could result in another leg down"
for financial markets, Mr. Quinlan adds. In that case, he says, the one bit of good news is that the Fed already
has pushed its target overnight-lending rate up to 5.25%. In case of bad economic trouble, the Fed has room to
cut rates sharply and stimulate the economy.
