Oil Futures Pass $77 a Barrel, And $80 Seems the Next Step
Commodities Report
Oil Futures Pass $77 a Barrel, And $80 Seems the Next Step
By Masood Farivar
489 words
15 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude-oil futures pierced $77 a barrel during floor trading, as continued violence in the Middle East kept the
specter of a disruption in oil supply on the horizon.
The August crude contract on the New York Mercantile Exchange ended 33 cents higher at $77.03 a barrel -- its
highest settlement from the open-outcry session, where traders shout buy and sell orders.
The contract set an exchange-record high of $78.40 a barrel in electronic trading. August gasoline, meanwhile,
gained 2.36 cents to $2.3249 a gallon.
Crude prices gained 4% on the week and are up almost a third so far this year.
Friday's gain was the fourth straight for oil, and the 13th in the last 16 sessions. Crude oil has surged 12% during
the 16-day run. However, it is still more than $20 away from the inflation-adjusted record set in April 1980 of
$98.58 a barrel.
Year-to-date, crude-oil futures are up 26%.
The failure by crude to edge higher still was attributed by analysts to preweekend profit-taking and not a sign of
any ease in worries over the conflict in the Middle East.
"We've seen a lot of bad news this week, and I think the odds of getting more bad news over the weekend are as
great as the odds of getting some favorable news," said Jim Ritterbusch, president of Ritterbusch &Co. in Galena,
Ill.
Barring a dramatic lessening of the violence over the weekend, analysts expect oil prices to extend their race to
uncharted territory.
"Eighty dollars is a done deal," said Peter Beutel, president of trading advisory firm Cameron Hanover in New
Canaan, Conn.
Crude futures for delivery later this year and next year traded at above that milestone for the first time.
With the front-month August crude contract due to expire in the coming week, the September contract "could get
up to where the winter contracts traded," Mr. Ritterbusch said.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose due to geopolitical concerns in
the Middle East and rising crude-oil prices. Nearby July gold climbed $13.70 to $666.60 an ounce. Gold is $53.20,
or 7.4%, off of the 26-year high it hit on May 11 but is still up 29% on the year-to-date.
COPPER: Comex prices drew support from continuing labor issues at copper mines as well as from higher
crude-oil and precious-metals futures. Much of the market's focus remains on a continuing strike against Grupo
Mexico and potential for a strike against Chile's Escondida, the world's largest copper mine, when a contract with
union workers expires Aug. 2, market insiders said. July copper rose 3.8 cents to $3.8295 a pound.

Dow Falls 1.5% as Oil Hits High
Thursday's Markets
Dow Falls 1.5% as Oil Hits High
By Jason Leow
730 words
14 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ANOTHER SURGE in oil prices to an exchange record and escalating tensions in the Middle East combined to hit
stocks hard again.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average plunged 166.89 points, or 1.5%, to 10846.29. It was the third 100-plus-point
fall in the past five trading sessions, and yesterday's losses also constituted the second half of the Dow's worst
two-day drubbing since the middle of May.
The Dow is still up more than 1% for the year, but the possible fallout from soaring energy prices has market
watchers fearing the start of another extended pullback and even a possible economic downturn.
Among other indexes, the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 16.31 points, or 1.3%, to 1242.29, down 0.48%
in 2006. The tech-heavy Nasdaq suffered earnings worries, slipping 36.13 points, or about 1.7%, to 2054.11,
down nearly 7% this year.
"It isn't inflation," said Tobias Levkovich, chief U.S. equity strategist with Citigroup. "It's more the fear of a growth
[slowdown]."
On the New York Mercantile Exchange, the near-month crude-oil futures contract gained $1.75 a barrel to $76.70,
the highest settlement ever for Nymex oil, and surpassing the July 5 exchange record of $75.19. Oil has jumped
26% this year.
Still, oil prices remain well below the 1980 record high when adjusted for inflation, which is $98.58 a barrel in May
2006 dollars. (Some traders see oil at $80.)
Benjamin Pace, chief investment officer at Deutsche Bank Private Wealth Management, noted that the market
had been regrouping since the middle of last month, "but these geopolitical concerns have brought us back
down."
While the Nymex oil contract has been trending upward for months, renewed chaos in the oil-rich Middle East
adds pressure to the supply side of the global energy equation. Israel and Lebanon traded attacks. Meanwhile,
assaults on two pipelines in Nigeria further unnerved energy traders.
A related concern: whether such price pressures will discourage the Federal Reserve from halting its long
rate-raising campaign to ward off inflation. The Fed has increased its target for short-term interest rates in each of
its past 17 policy-making meetings. That target, the fed-funds rate, stands at 5.25%.
The Bank of Japan also is expected to raise its interest rates today for the first time in six years. While lending
terms in that country will remain low compared with the rest of the world, rising rates there could further
discourage borrowing for investment in stocks and other asset markets.
Also weighing on the broader market: Brokerage-firm downgrades on two widely held stocks. Merrill Lynch cut its
recommendation on Wal-Mart Stores to "neutral" from "buy," sending that big-box retailer's shares down over 2%.
And Walt Disney shares lost more than 4% after CIBC World Markets downgraded the media company to "sector
underperformer" from "sector performer."
Only three Dow components -- Home Depot, Merck and Exxon Mobil -- finished in the black.

Tech stocks were pulled down after SAP, the big German software maker with many U.S. customers, reported
below-forecast sales. Its stock fell 7% and dragged down rival Oracle, whose stock declined nearly 1%.

Outside the U.S., stocks fell in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. shares, was down

euro fell against the dollar to $1.2688, from 1.2701.

on concerns about North Korea's nuclear program.
The Bush administration said it holds Syria and Iran, "which directly support Hezbollah," responsible for the
attack. Israeli Prime Minister Ehud Olmert called the soldiers' capture "an act of war," and his Cabinet prepared to
approve more military action in Lebanon.
"The unrest in the Middle East is really something we need to keep an eye on. It could spiral out of control if Israel
were to attack Syria," said Edward Meir, an analyst at brokerage Man Financial in Chicago.
Adding to geopolitical concerns in the Middle East, Iran, the second-biggest oil exporter in the Organization of
Petroleum Exporting Countries, will be referred back to the U.N. Security Council over its nuclear program,
French foreign minister Philippe Douste-Blazy said.
Recent tensions with Iran have boosted oil prices against a backdrop of concerns that the Islamic nation could
use oil as a negotiating weapon or cut off access to other crude from the Middle East.
Earlier in the day, the market gyrated after the Energy Department released its closely watched weekly data. The
statistics showed crude-oil stockpiles fell by a greater-than-expected six million barrels to 335.3 million barrels in
the week ended July 7, as imports plunged more than one million barrels a day. Analysts had expected a draw of
1.4 million barrels.
Gasoline stockpiles fell by 400,000 barrels, which was slightly less than market expectations, to 212.7 million
barrels. Demand for the week remained relatively strong at 9.6 million barrels a day.
In other commodity markets:

bullish technical chart points and safe-haven buying as tensions mount following Tuesday's railway bombing in
Mumbai. Most-active August gold gained $8.10 an ounce to $651.20. July gained $8.20 to $649.40.

2006-07 cotton crop and supportive data from the Agriculture Department suggesting a smaller crop. December,
which represents the fall harvest, gained 1.06 cents to 53.71 cents per pound.

Dollar Climbs Against Rivals Amid Wait for Japan Rate Moves
Currency Trading
Dollar Climbs Against Rivals Amid Wait for Japan Rate Moves
By Chuck Marvin
537 words
11 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar overcame an initial lag against the yen to end active trading higher versus both its Japanese rival and
the euro as investors speculated on a possible increase in interest rates by the Bank of Japan later this week.
The dollar rode the coattails of a selloff of the euro against the yen, with the common currency falling to 145.07
yen from 146.06 yen before smoothing out near 145.40 yen.
Late in the New York afternoon, the euro was at $1.2737 from $1.2815 late Friday. The dollar was at 114.18 yen
from 113.93 yen late Friday, while the euro was at 145.46 yen from 146.06 yen. The dollar was at 1.2303 Swiss
francs from 1.2223 francs late Friday, and sterling was at $1.8417 from $1.8518.
The yen entered the week on a strong note ahead of Friday's Bank of Japan meeting on interest rates. Japan has
maintained a long-running 0% overnight interbank lending policy, and many analysts expect the central bank to
increase rates as soon as Friday.
The yen was buoyed by data released overnight that suggested Japanese bank lending grew at its fastest pace in
over a decade in June. Lending by Japanese banks rose 1.8% in June from a year earlier to 382.889 trillion yen,
marking the fifth straight month of increases and much higher than a 1.2% increase in May. It was the biggest
increase since March 1996, the Bank of Japan said.
Companies are borrowing more money from banks to increase capital expenditure, while optimism for the
economy is keeping demand for housing loans on the rise, a Bank of Japan official said at a briefing for reporters
in Tokyo.
Also supporting the yen overnight were industrial data that beat market expectations. While Japanese core
machinery orders fell on month in May because of declines in the manufacturing sector, the slump was milder
than expected, indicating that corporate spending, which has been an engine of economic growth, remains in a
healthy upward trend for now.
According to Sophia Drossos, currency strategist at Morgan Stanley in New York, traders chose to sell the euro in
exchange for yen because the euro has been trading at historical highs and provided an opportunity for
profit-taking.
Elsewhere, the Canadian dollar was hurt overnight, as analysts said fears surfaced that the central bank might
not announce an interest-rate increase today. Thanks to a weaker-than-expected jobs report Friday in Canada,
the Canadian dollar has been pressured.
"There's a little bit of nervousness over the Bank of Canada," said Steve Butler, director of foreign exchange at
Scotia Capital in Toronto. "And after Friday's jobs data, it looks like the market got caught leaning the wrong way
and pushing a bit too hard at the wrong levels."
Late in the afternoon, the U.S. dollar was trading at C$1.1251 from C$1.1142 late Friday. A decline in prices for
commodities such as gold and oil also weighed on the Canadian dollar.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...And Not: Interest-Rate Increases Abroad Could Weigh Down U.S. Markets --- Overseas...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...And Not: Interest-Rate Increases Abroad Could
Weigh Down U.S. Markets --- Overseas Activity, Not Fed, Has Driven Investors Into Seeking Safer Plays
By Peter A. McKay
674 words
10 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
INVESTORS' EXPECTATIONS regarding the supply of "easy money" have shifted markedly since late May,
spurring moves into and out of a variety of investments globally.
Large-cap U.S. stocks, for example, have fared better than technology shares. Foreign stocks in countries where
interest rates are expected to rise are lagging. With the exception of crude oil, most commodity prices have fallen
in the last five weeks.
The U.S. Federal Reserve's interest-rate moves have garnered the most attention, with expectations that
policymakers will soon end a two-year spate of rate increases that brought the benchmark short-term rate to
5.25%.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average is almost unchanged since late May but is up more than 3% from its mid-June
lows, closing at 11090.67 Friday. Other major U.S. indicators have followed a similar pattern.
However, rate-setters in other countries, notably Japan, seem headed in the opposite direction from the U.S. Fed.
Many analysts believe Japanese policymakers at a scheduled meeting Friday could raise their key interest rate
for the first time in more than five years. With such expectations mounting, the Nikkei 225-stock index has fallen
more than 3% since late May, or 551.84 points, to 15307.61.
Generally, higher interest rates make bonds more attractive than stocks because of their guaranteed return. In
effect, higher rates also take money out of circulation within a country's economy, leaving less of it available to
flow into the stock market.
For now, that is particularly important to U.S. stock and bond markets, which in recent years have seen increasing
participation from foreign investors. Hence, rate decisions elsewhere can affect how much is going into U.S.
assets.
"A lot of the story in the stock market right now is not about the Fed," said New York-based strategist Tobias
Levkovich, of Citigroup. "To a large extent, the overseas markets are driving us, not the other way around."
Since the end of May, the Dow Jones World Stock Index excluding U.S. shares has edged up 0.6%, or 1.17
points, to 213.85.
With the rate outlook in flux, investors have been seeking less risky investments, which has hobbled indicators
that track technology and small-capitalization companies. The Russell 2000, for instance, has slipped 0.2%, or
1.74 points to 709.30 since the end of May. The Nasdaq Composite Index has shed 1.6%, or 34.68 points, to
2130.06.
"There's a mindset out there right now that the (stock) market is horrible," said Wells Capital Management
strategist Jim Paulsen. "I don't think that mindset has caught up yet" with the benefits that seem imminent once
the Fed stops tightening.
One threat to stocks that has reemerged lately is high oil prices, which cut into consumers' discretionary spending
and affect corporate profits. Since the end of May, crude futures have risen 2.9%, or $2.06, to $74.09 a barrel on
the New York Mercantile Exchange.

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Geopolitical worries, isolated supply disruptions and strong demand have pushed prices higher. For example, the
the U.S. Department of Energy recently said gasoline demand hit its second-highest weekly average prior to the
Independence Day holiday.
Other commodity prices haven't fared as well. The broad-based Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index is down 2.1%,
or 3.686 points, to 174.966, since late May.
Gold futures are off 3.2%, or $21.10, to $632.80 a troy ounce on Nymex's Comex division.
Traditionally, the metal has moved in the opposite direction of the U.S. dollar. Since late May, the J.P. Morgan
trade-weighted index tracking the dollar's value against a basket of foreign currencies is up 0.3%, or 0.3 point, to
88.3.

For the first time since the last bear market ended in 2002, a broad swath of professional investors are

They are moving away from the high-flying investments that have rewarded them handsomely for years and are
now seeking out something they had come to despise: quality.
Brett Gallagher, co-head of global stock investment at Julius Baer Investment Management in New York, is
dumping once-trendy stocks from far-flung places like India, betting instead on developing economies with what
he considers better long-term prospects, including Poland's. He's avoiding regional banks with lots of exposure to
consumer loans, preferring bigger global players. Also off his radar: anything favored by fast-money hedge funds
-- "renters, not owners," he scoffs. Instead, he likes more staid stocks, such as General Electric Co.
"It is clear which stocks have an investor base and which are powered by near-term speculation," Mr. Gallagher
says. "For anyone who is an investor as opposed to a renter of stocks, upgrading to quality in the portfolio is
going to pay benefits."
Money managers are concluding that it's time to cut back on their holdings of the volatile and increasingly
speculative investments that until recently had been soaring -- gold, industrial commodities, real estate as well as
small stocks and many developing-country stocks.
"Starting in 2002 and 2003, it was 'the riskier the investment the better.' Now, the tide has shifted," says Jeff
Schappe, chief investment officer of BB&T Corp.'s BB&T Asset Management in Raleigh, North Carolina.
As the era of cheap borrowing ends and the economic climate grows more conservative, investment pros are
betting that higher-quality investments will fare better in a slower economy -- and suffer less if there's a bear
market. But they don't all define "quality" in the same way. Here is a look at some of their strategies:
Pick Less Volatile Stocks
Money manager Janna Sampson of OakBrook Investments in Lisle, Ill., decided it was time to adjust her
investment approach in May, after CNBC anchor Maria Bartiromo reported on an off-the-cuff remark made by
Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke at a May social gathering in Washington. He told the journalist the
market was wrong in its perception that the Fed was poised to stop raising rates.
"It was after Bernanke made his now-famous comments at the White House Correspondents Dinner," Ms.
Sampson recalls. After that, "we started to see every Fed president and every Fed board member making more
hawkish comments."
Ms. Sampson trimmed holdings of stocks such as McDonald's Corp. and Waste Management Inc. that were up
sharply and could fare less well if economic growth slowed. She cut back on H.J. Heinz Co., because it was up on
speculative buying, not on fundamentals, after an activist investor pressed for strategic changes. She shifted
money into stocks that haven't been as sought-after and for which expectations are lower, such as Kimberly-Clark
Corp. and Microsoft Corp.
In April, Susan Malley, president of Malley Associates Capital Management in New York, pulled back from the
bets she had made on economic growth last year. She reduced holdings in smaller companies and in those

--- Page 155 ---
Page 155 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
closely tied to economic expansion. Among them: oil drillers such as Diamond Offshore Drilling Inc., and
technology companies like communications-chip maker Broadcom Corp., and software maker Cognos Inc.
She shifted toward larger oil-industry stocks, including Schlumberger Ltd. and Baker Hughes Inc. and boosted
exposure to companies with steady sales streams, such as PepsiCo Inc., Kellogg Co., CVS Corp. and Procter
&Gamble Co. Although they tended to be laggards until recently, these so-called Big Uglies look safer and more
stable now, she says.
A typical account at her firm went to 14% tech stocks, down from 18% at the end of March. "We have reduced
them not because we don't like them but because we want a little less volatility," she says.
Mr. Gallagher, of Julius Baer, notes that stocks such as U.S. Steel Corp., Apple Computer Inc., copper-producer
Phelps Dodge Corp. and chip-maker Advanced Micro Devices Inc. all have seen rapid buying and selling in
recent months. He sees that as a sure sign of excessive interest by hedge funds, the fast-trading investment
pools that cater mainly to deep-pocketed investors.
Because fickle hedge funds can suddenly dump large amounts of shares -- making stock prices fall sharply -- he
is bullish on stocks in which trading has been quieter. He likes GE, United Parcel Service Inc. and PepsiCo --
companies whose fundamentals he believes make them good longterm prospects.
Beware Consumer Stocks
BB&T's Mr. Schappe has a secret weapon to track consumer demand: his father, who sells real estate in
mobile-home parks in Punta Gorda, on Florida's west coast.
"A year ago, we liked consumer discretionary stocks a lot more," Mr. Schappe says. But his research showed
loan delinquencies on the rise, and his father reported that his customers' buying power was weakening.
To cut its exposure to discretionary stocks -- those that rise and fall with consumer spending on nonessential
goods -- BB&T trimmed holdings of handbag retailer Coach Inc. and women's clothing retailer Chico's FAS Inc. It
also pared holdings of computer-chip makers such as Broadcom and National Semiconductor Corp.
"Consumers have pretty much tapped out of their home equity, and that cash register has shown signs of going
silent," he says. "We are tending to focus on businesses that sell to businesses." To that end, he boosted
investments in companies such as Boeing Co., Oracle Corp. and Caterpillar Inc. -- companies with large-scale
contracts and good global growth prospects.
Henry Herrmann, chief executive of money-management firm Waddell &Reed in Overland Park, Kan., says pros
there have recently sold some stocks and boosted cash reserves. "I am not thinking that this is all over yet," he
says of the declines in stock prices.
As his money managers gradually put cash to work, they're buying U.S.-based infrastructure stocks -- linked to
energy, industry and construction.
Mr. Gallagher of Julius Baer is staying away from regional banks, for fear of bad consumer loans. "We prefer the
big diversified companies that have overseas earnings," such as Citigroup, he says.
Strategist Joseph Quinlan at Bank of America's global wealth and investment-management division is telling
clients to focus on capital-goods companies and infrastructure builders. Such businesses stand to benefit from
continued, even if slowed, economic growth. He has calculated that big banks, brokerage firms and insurance
companies as a group now receive 25% of their revenue from abroad, insulating them somewhat from the U.S.
consumer.
Think Big, Not Small
The Russell 2000 small-stock index more than doubled during the current bull market, hitting repeated record
highs, while the Dow Jones Industrial Average rose roughly half as much and never hit a record. Now, the tables
may be turning. Since peaking in May, the Russell is down 7.8%, while the Dow has fallen only 3.6%.
Small stocks typically do best when economic growth is accelerating, because small companies are more nimble
and can grow faster. But they also tend to be more dependent on bank loans, making them suffer more when
rates rise and economic growth slows.

--- Page 156 ---
Page 156 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"If you are at the start of an economic boom, smaller companies might have more room to run. But that isn't what
we are seeing now," says Marc Stern, chief investment officer at Bessemer Investment Management in New
York.
For some time, analysts have been urging investors to return to once-popular big stocks that had languished and
become cheap, and now some investors are listening to that advice.
"A good proxy for quality is size," says Mr. Schappe of BB&T. Even fund managers at his firm who specialize in
small stocks, he says, "are buying larger small stocks."
Large stocks, says Ms. Sampson of OakBrook, often are better protected from difficult markets -- "by patents,
strong brands or barriers to entry."
But not everyone is persuaded that the small-stock run is done. Small stocks have become particular favorites of
hedge funds, precisely because they can rise more sharply when they become hot, says Mr. Herrmann of
Waddell &Reed. And hedge funds represent as much as half of stock trading on some trading days.
Seek Quality Foreign Markets
Among the worst places to be over the past two months have been what investors call "emerging markets" --
stock markets of developing countries. India, for example, is down 15% from its recent high, in May. Colombia is
down 29% from a January high, and in the Philippines the stock market is off 14% from its May peak.
"Emerging markets have become the tech stocks of this decade," says Russ Koesterich, senior portfolio manager
at Barclays Global Investors in San Francisco.
Nevertheless, investors such as Julius Baer's Mr. Gallagher are using the downdraft as a chance to buy back in
and upgrade to better quality in the process.
Mr. Gallagher has been selling stocks in countries "that are less good fundamentally, and taking the opportunity to
buy those where we think the long-term story is still in place," he says.
One problem: Not everyone can agree on which countries offer the best investment quality. Mr. Gallagher favors
Eastern Europe because he sees continuing development without inflation or Asia's dependency on exports.
"Asia is very much dependent on the U.S. consumer. To the extent that the U.S. consumer stumbles, these
markets will be more affected," he says. He dislikes Mexico, which he says is losing exports to China, and likes
Brazil for its strong domestic market.
Mr. Quinlan of Bank of America, who urged clients to get out of emerging markets earlier this year, was largely
ignored then, he says. Now, he is telling them to start selectively buying again. But scared off by the recent
declines, they still aren't listening. He sees quality in more-advanced economies such as South Korea, Brazil,
Hong Kong and Singapore, but not in Turkey or India, where he sees inflation risks.
Plenty of people remain skeptical of emerging markets in general, preferring Japan and Western Europe.
"We have a saying in the firm: An emerging market is one you can't emerge from in an emergency," says Richard
Steinberg, president of Steinberg Global Asset Management in Boca Raton, Fla.
Find a Shield From the Dollar
With investors hoping that the Federal Reserve can stop raising U.S. interest rates some time this summer, and
with governments in Europe and Asia sending signals that they will boost rates in the future, investors are starting
to think the dollar could come under attack again.
The dollar is affected by a wide variety of factors, from trade flows to world demand for U.S. investments. The
dollar tends to be stronger when U.S. interest rates are high compared with foreign rates, since higher interest
rates make the dollar more attractive to hold.
That's why many pros are looking for ways to invest abroad, and why they are looking for other ways to bet
against the dollar as well.
Mr. Steinberg is betting that the euro will rise again. He is buying an exchange-traded fund that tracks the euro's
value. He thinks the dollar could weaken ahead of U.S. elections, especially if the Fed signals an intention to stop
raising U.S. rates.

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We think the thing to do with all this turbulence in the dollar is to keep a higher allocation to foreign markets,"
says Peter Wall, chief investment officer at Chase Private Client Services, a brokerage and money-management
arm of J.P. Morgan Chase.
But not everyone is so sure. "Money is coming back to the dollar," says Mr. Herrmann of Waddell &Reed. He
thinks nervousness about foreign investments could keep money at home and prevent the dollar from falling too
hard.
To profit from any dollar decline without taking much risk, some investors are simply focusing on large
multinationals that derive profits in many currencies. If the dollar weakens, their offshore income rises. If it
doesn't, investors still benefit from the stocks' overall quality.
Bob Bissell, president of Wells Capital Management, a money-management arm of Wells Fargo, fears U.S.
stocks could remain in the doldrums this summer. "Now, people are being paid to wait -- they are getting a good
return in money-market funds," he says.

Return of Rate Woes, Higher Oil Prices Help Push DJIA Down 76.20 Points
Wednesday's Markets
Return of Rate Woes, Higher Oil Prices Help Push DJIA Down 76.20 Points
By E.S. Browning
558 words
6 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
RESURGENT INTEREST-RATE worries, together with missile news and higher oil prices, took some of the
recent optimism out of the stock market, knocking down the Dow Jones Industrial Average.
After rising last week and on Monday amid hopes for an end to Federal Reserve interest-rate increases, the Dow
industrials gave back almost all of Monday's 77.80-point gain, falling 76.20 points, or 0.68%, to 11151.82. The
Dow is still up 4.1% this year.
Interest-rate hopes suffered after Automatic Data Processing, which tracks payrolls, estimated that more jobs
than anticipated were created in June. If government data due tomorrow confirm that, it will indicate a stronger
economy, boosting pressure on the Fed to again raise rates in order to prevent inflation.
The fear of rising rates is bad for stocks and bonds. Higher rates crimp corporate profits and makes existing
bonds, with their lower rates, less valuable. Treasury-bond prices sank yesterday, pushing bond yields higher.
Also, news of North Korea's missile test gave many investors a jolt, making some pull back from stocks. The
uncertainty over world security also helped push crude-oil futures up $1.26 to $75.19 a barrel, the highest finish
for a nearby month contract since they began trading on the New York Mercantile Exchange. Adjusted for inflation,
however, oil has traded as high as $98.58 in 1980, which was before crude futures were available on the Nymex.
Higher oil prices crimp consumer spending and business profits.
"Altogether, it was just too much to digest," said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at Cowen &Co. "We had a
nice Fourth of July rally and we just gave that back."
Foreign stocks also fell, and gold futures, a refuge in times of uncertainty, rose $13.80 to $627.30 a troy ounce.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.72%, or 9.28 points, to 1270.91, still up 1.8% for 2006. The
Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks can be volatile in uncertain times, fell 1.69%, or 37.09
points, to 2153.34, down 2.4% on the year.
Investors fear more ups and downs, since the next Fed meeting isn't until Aug. 8, leaving room for a lot more
economic data before the central bank makes its next rate decision. Investors also will be digesting quarterly
corporate-profit reports due in increasing numbers starting next week.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, fell
1.78%, or 3.83 points, to 211.43.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.16 billion shares,
as 923 stocks rose and 2,426 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 18/32, or $5.63 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
up to 5.224%. The 30-year bond was down 29/32 to yield 5.270%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 115.71 yen, up from 114.74, while the euro fell against the
dollar to $1.2729 from $1.2800.

Crude-Oil Prices Top $75 a Barrel On Demand, Mideast Worries
Commodities Report
Crude-Oil Prices Top $75 a Barrel On Demand, Mideast Worries
By Bhushan Bahree
764 words
6 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
With the U.S. and China continuing to guzzle more oil and potential supply outages brewing in the Middle East
and Africa, crude-oil prices surged past $75 a barrel to an exchange record yesterday.
U.S. benchmark crude for August delivery traded as high as $75.40 a barrel during the day, an intraday record for
nearby-month oil futures on the New York Mercantile Exchange, before settling $1.26 higher at $75.19, also an
exchange record for a nearby-month settlement price. That said, oil prices have been more than $20 higher than
this in the past in inflation-adjusted terms. Yesterday's closing figure exceeded by two cents the previous Nymex
record high, set April 21.
Industry analysts said threats to oil supply from places as far apart as Iran and Nigeria continue even as demand
for oil rises. A seasonal dip in oil demand during spring in the Northern Hemisphere is now over. Typically,
demand starts rising in July as, often, do prices.
"You don't want to be caught short in a market like this," said Roger Diwan, an analyst at PFC Energy, a
Washington consulting firm.
Indeed, some of yesterday's gains came as investors returning from the long July 4th trading break started the
new quarter by bidding up commodity futures, convinced that rising demand and increasing threats to world oil
supplies will lead to higher prices later in the year.
"The new quarter has brought the risk capital out of hiding," said Phil Flynn, an analyst at Alaron Trading Corp. in
Chicago. "Funds that took profits or fled from the market on fears of rising interest rates are now looking around to
see where the best place to make money is."
Mr. Diwan and others reckon crude-oil prices are headed even higher in the days and weeks ahead. The reason:
Recent expectations that demand might fall because of high prices have been dispelled, said Antoine Halff, an
analyst at Fimat USA, the brokerage arm of France's Societe Generale. "I think we might pass $80 [a barrel]," Mr.
Halff said.
Part of the price surge of the past week is ascribed to technical factors in the market, which had fluctuated
between $68 a barrel and $72 a barrel for some time before breaking out toward $75 a barrel, a so-called
resistance level for investors and traders.
Equally important are the signs of a return of bullish sentiment on oil based on preliminary demand data. Adam
Sieminski, an analyst at Deutsche Bank AG in New York, said Chinese demand in May appeared to be about
10% ahead of a year ago. He also noted that based on preliminary reports by motoring and other organizations,
traders may have concluded that gasoline use was high during the July 4 holiday weekend in the U.S. If
confirmed, the burst of driving would reduce U.S. inventories of gasoline at a time when refiners are experiencing
operational problems and their capacity to process crude oil into usable products like gasoline and diesel is
already stretched world-wide.
Gasoline prices on the Nymex ended at $2.2758 a gallon, up 5.7 cents. Retail gasoline prices typically are some
65 cents higher than the exchange price.
The Energy Department is due to announce its closely watched weekly snapshot of oil use and inventories today.

--- Page 165 ---
Page 165 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The U.S. is by far the world's largest gasoline market, using some 9.4 million barrels a day. World-wide
consumption of all oil products is about 85 million barrels a day, with the U.S. accounting for about a quarter.
China is the world's second-largest oil consumer, followed by Japan.
In other commodity trading:
GOLD: Futures on the Comex division of Nymex rose, helped by safe-haven buying after news of North Korea's
missile tests. Other supportive influences included continuing concerns about Iran's nuclear program and
comments from the United Arab Emirates' central bank governor saying the country may add to its gold reserves.
Nearby July gold rose $13.80 an ounce to $627.30, while August gold gained $13.70 to $629.70.
WHEAT: Prices on the Minneapolis Grain Exchange rose to contract highs of $5.22 a bushel, supported by
concerns over further deterioration in the hard red spring crop of the northern Plains amid forecasts for hot, dry
weather to carry into next week. Nearby July rose 10 cents to settle at $5.20.
---

Stock Market Quarterly Review: Raw-Material Pullback Catches Some Off Guard
Stock Market Quarterly Review: Raw-Material Pullback Catches Some Off Guard
By Ann Davis
1,114 words
3 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
COMMODITIES FANS who went on auto-pilot in the first half of 2006, leaving money in a broad basket of raw
materials as prices soared, came in for a let down by June.
Many probably wish they had followed the stock-market adage, "sell in May, and go away." Prices of commodity
futures contracts from copper and zinc to wheat skyrocketed in May, then pulled back sharply.
While many commodities still chalked up price gains for the second quarter, the popular strategy of putting funds
in so-called passive commodities investments -- indexes and exchange-traded funds that are backed by a
predetermined mix of commodities futures contracts -- produced mediocre returns.
Oil finished the quarter up 11% at $73.93 a barrel, and unleaded gasoline for July delivery settled at $2.2021 a
gallon, up 13% on the New York Mercantile Exchange. In the industrial metals sector, even with a big correction
in May, copper finished 39.2% higher for the quarter at $3.4625 a pound on the Comex division of the Nymex.
Precious metals had a mixed finish, with gold up 5.5% at $613.50 an ounce and silver dropping 5.6% to $10.833
an ounce. Corn was flat, down just 0.2% in the second quarter to $2.355 a bushel, though it was up 9.5%
year-to-date, and wheat rose 7.1% to $3.715 a bushel at the Chicago Board of Trade.
Kevin Norrish, commodities analyst at Barclays Capital, the securities unit of Barclays PLC, sees energy and
agriculture gaining this year and cautions against piling into industrial and precious metals after many hit a peak
May 11. "We expect metals to underperform [relative to other commodities], because we think that energy and
agriculture will be much less affected by inflation and growth concerns over the next few months," he says.
There is a lot at stake. Institutional money managers -- representing mutual funds, pensions and endowments --
have $100 billion to $120 billion in commodities, up from $6 billion in 1999, with $80 billion of that in index-based
investments, estimates Barclays Capital. Several billion dollars has flowed into commodities indexes just in past
few months, index operators say.
For the latest three-month period, many indexes meant to capture the quarter's moves had comparatively lower
gains. The energy-heavy Goldman Sachs Commodity Index rose 6.7% as measured by its total return, a metric
showing how money invested according to the index's rules performed. The Reuters/Jefferies CRB Index total
return was 5.2%. The Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index of 19 commodities posted a total return of 6.1%. Total
returns adjust for the cost of regularly selling expiring futures contracts and buying new ones and thus differ from
so-called "spot" returns, which track the current prices of commodities.
One culprit bringing down the averages was natural gas, which is off 15.3% for the quarter and 45.6% for the year
to date after a mild winter led to unusually high inventories of the fuel, used to heat about 60% of U.S. homes.
Natural gas finished the quarter at $6.104 a British thermal unit, down from $7.21 on March 31. Natural gas
constitutes 3% to 12% of most widely followed commodities indexes.
A more significant factor behind the index numbers is what is known as "roll return," which is the gain or loss
resulting from rolling over expiring futures contracts into new ones. This has been a drag on oil in particular,
especially after oil prices stopped climbing in the second quarter of 2006 and settled into a relatively stable range
of $70 to $72.
Futures contracts set the price for delivery of a commodity sometime in the future. Continuously rolling over
expiring contracts is a way many investors maintain an investment in commodities such as oil without taking or
making delivery. In the past year and a half, oil prices have become higher in future-delivery months, so replacing

--- Page 169 ---
Page 169 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
expiring contracts with more expensive ones significantly hurts returns. In the past, oil-contract prices were lower
in future-delivery months.
A few years ago, this enabled investors to reap a profit in the oil markets even if prices weren't moving, says Tom
Price, managing member of Beeland Management Co., the general partner of several commodities funds that
track the Rogers International Commodities Index, which has a 35% weighting in crude oil. When the spot price
was $30 a barrel, he estimates investors rolling their contracts were able to buy a new contract for about 60 cents
less a barrel than the expiring month's contract. He estimates that would have added 8.4 percentage points to the
Rogers index's return no matter where commodity prices went.
The amount varies, but these days investors have to pay about 90 cents a barrel more when they replace the
contract they are selling. Assuming that penalty stays at 90 cents all year, it would add up to a drag of nearly six
percentage points on the Rogers index's annual return, Mr. Price calculates.
In a market such as the current one, the price of oil has to rise significantly to compensate for the negative roll
return. In 2005, says John Shapiro, head of commodities at Morgan Stanley, the 46% increase in oil prices did a
lot "to compensate for the fact that the rolls were done at negative numbers." Today, with prices rising less
rapidly, he says, "energy has a lot of hills to climb to overcome" the price structure.
The Rogers index, which has a weighting in various energy commodities of just 44%, compared with the GSCI's
75% energy concentration, had a total return of about 4.5%.The Deutsche Bank Liquid Commodity Index returned
nearly 6%. Although the Deutsche Bank index doesn't hold natural gas, a big loser in 2006, it has a 15%
weighting in aluminum, which rose 4.5% in the quarter on the London Metals Exchange. Dow Jones-AIG index's
mix of agricultural holdings helped hold down its return, along with a 12% weighting in natural gas.
Deutsche Bank AG introduced an index designed to minimize the negative roll return by replacing contracts in a
more flexible way. Michael Lewis, head of commodities research at Deutsche Bank, explains that "instead of
every single month buying the next month's contract, Deutsche Bank will make a decision on which contract is the
best one to roll into at maturity."
Many investment banks are urging investors to consider tailor-made commodities transactions to help blunt the
problem.

Stock Market Quarterly Review -- Abreast of the Market: Investors Are Taken on Wild Ride
Stock Market Quarterly Review -- Abreast of the Market: Investors Are Taken on Wild Ride
By E.S. Browning
1,607 words
3 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER A CALM, strong first three months of the year, stocks gave investors whiplash in the second quarter,
leaving the experts debating how much more trouble could be in the offing.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average was up more than 8% for the year on May 10, less than 100 points short of a
record. Then it began gyrating, putting in 100-point declines and advances in rapid succession, as investors
debated the market's outlook. The Dow industrials wound up giving back half of the year's gains. The average
finished Friday at 11150.22, up 40.90 points, or 0.37%, for the quarter and up 4% for 2006. That left it 572.76
points short of the record 11722.98, which dates from January 2000.
In more than seven years, since mid-1999, blue-chip stocks as a group haven't done more than move sideways --
and most technology stocks, which suffered most in the long bear market, are languishing well below their
records. Many analysts caution that any stock-market gains this year could be single-digit at best.
Behind the market's uncertainty is Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke, who took office on Feb. 1 and
began issuing statements that took some investors by surprise. After initially indicating he was looking for a way
to pause in the Fed's two-year-old campaign of interest-rate increases, Mr. Bernanke changed his tune in May.
He began warning that he was seeing signs of inflationary pressures that could keep the rate increases coming.
Then, after the Fed's latest policy meeting Thursday, he changed again. The Fed issued a statement hinting that
it is looking for a way to take a break from rate boosts.
Following Thursday's meeting on interest-rate policy, the Fed announced that "additional firming" (Fedspeak for
more rate increases) will depend on "incoming information," suggesting once again that the Fed is hoping to avoid
raising rates at its next meeting in August, if economic data suggest inflation is under control. After the May
meeting, the Fed's statement had warned that further "policy firming," might "yet be needed."
Stock investors fixate on interest-rate news because, historically, rising rates often have been what has killed bull
markets. Higher rates are bad for stocks because they raise business costs and squeeze profits. They make
alternative investments, such as money-market funds, more attractive.
Even if the Fed manages to pause in its rate increases, authorities in Europe and Asia have indicated that they
are going to continue raising rates, as well as increase reserve requirements for banks and take other steps to
make money less freely available. The world-wide tightening of monetary policy has made investors more
cautious. Throughout the world, they had used cheap money to buy stocks and other financial investments. Now,
feeling the pinch of tighter money, they have pulled back from some riskier investments.
The volatile Nasdaq Composite Index, dominated by technology stocks, had been up more than 7% for the year
at its high on April 19. It gave up all those gains and finished Friday at 2172.09, down 7.17% for the quarter and
down 1.5% for the year. The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index finished June at 1270.20, down 1.9% for the
quarter but still up 1.8% for 2006.
Many analysts see the current pullback as part of a simple "correction" -- a decline of about 10% from a high --
rather than the 20% drop that would signal a bear market. Many thought such a correction was overdue in a bull
market that hadn't seen a 10% decline since 2003.
Some, especially technical analysts who chart market trends rather than analyzing fundamental business factors
such as profits and sales, warn that the bull market, which began nearly four years ago, is getting old and
extended, and that a bear market could be in the making.

--- Page 171 ---
Page 171 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Even fundamental analysts who don't see a bear market on the horizon have been warning clients that, with the
pace of profit gains slowing, the Dow industrials are likely to show no more than a single-digit percentage gain for
the current year. That would follow a nearly flat year in 2005, when the industrial average fell 0.61%.
"This is not some sort of big bear market now, it is just a correction," says Marc Stern, chief investment officer at
Bessemer Investment Management in New York. "We have been expecting that this year will be a modest-return
year for the stock market," meaning a single-digit rise, he says.
The end of the easy-money world has meant trouble for investments in a wide variety of areas that had come to
be viewed as risky, from developing-country stocks to industrial commodities to Florida condos to gold. All are off
sharply from their highs.
The declines in major U.S. stock indexes were small compared to those in other, more speculative areas. In India,
the benchmark stock index finished Friday down 16% from its recent high. In Colombia, it was down 33% and in
Turkey, 26%. In Japan, hardly a volatile emerging economy, the main stock index fell 12% from its recent high.
Gold finished the quarter at $613.50 a troy ounce, down 15% from the 26-year high of $719.80 it hit May 11.
Small U.S. stocks suffered, too. The Russell 2000 small-stock index, which had more than doubled from its 2002
low, was trading at a record in May, up 16% for the year. Since then, it has fallen 7.3%, despite a late-June
rebound. It finished Friday at 724.67, down 5.3% for the quarter but still up 7.6% for 2006.
"Money was almost free. Now rates are going up, and people are dumping risky assets," says Russ Koesterich,
senior portfolio manager at Barclays Global Investors in San Francisco. "To end this you need to remove the
cloud of uncertainty on monetary policy. Until that happens you are going to have volatility."
Worrisome to stock investors has been the run-up in bond yields. Treasury yields, especially on the 10-year note,
are benchmarks for a wide variety of other market interest rates, notably those of fixed-rate mortgages. For more
than a year, bond yields had remained persistently low despite the Fed's steady increase in short-term rates. By
helping to hold down the cost of money in the face of the Fed rate increases, low bond yields helped support the
economy and the stock market.
Starting this year, bond yields followed short-term rates higher, crimping economic growth, consumer spending
and corporate profits. The 10-year note's yield was as low as 4.3% in January. It pushed above 5% in April and
kept rising, finishing the quarter at 5.145%.
On top of that, prices of oil futures contracts have refused to fall significantly below $70 a barrel, despite signs of
a slowing U.S. economy, which has worsened worries about the impact of energy costs on consumer spending
and business profits.
All this has left investors cautious about the near-term outlook and reluctant to jump too heavily into some of the
speculative investments that had been magnets for investment until May.
"I'm not going to structure my portfolios to the idea that we are going to go back to what I would call pretty
significant speculative excess," says Henry Herrmann, chief executive officer at money-management firm Waddell
&Reed in Overland Park, Kan. "This has been a lesson in taking too much risk. I just feel we aren't going to be
quite as wild and crazy."
Adding to the concern this year is the Congressional election looming in November. Some investors worry that
stocks could remain volatile as investors bite their nails about the election prospects. In recent weeks, investors
have been circulating charts showing how stocks typically perform during the four-year "presidential cycle."
The charts show that the Dow industrials, on average, tend to hit headwinds in springtime of the second year of a
president's term, which would be this year. They typically don't bottom out until the fall, around the time of the
mid-term elections. Then they rebound, and in the following year show strong gains.
A study by Ned Davis Research in Venice, Fla., shows that, since the start of 2003, the Dow industrials have
followed the average pattern surprisingly closely, including the pullback in the spring of this year. Whether they
continue to follow the pattern could depend on a host of factors, including the Fed's interest-rate moves and the
direction of corporate profits.
The bulk of second-quarter profit reports are due from major companies by the end of this month. Although
companies no longer are turning in the 20% annual profit gains they recorded earlier in the economic recovery,
analysts are forecasting a 12th consecutive quarter of double-digit percentage gains. At some point, companies

--- Page 172 ---
Page 172 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
are going to have trouble delivering on analysts' increasingly bullish forecasts. Still, in recent quarters profits have
continued to outpace analyst expectations. That has helped keep stocks in positive territory this year.
"There's a higher degree of risk aversion now than conditions warrant," says Peter Wall, chief investment officer
for Chase Private Client Services, a brokerage and money-management arm of J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. "We
think stocks are fundamentally sound. But we still expect single-digit returns this year."

Crude-Oil Prices Extend Gains Amid Holiday Supply Concerns
Commodities Report
Crude-Oil Prices Extend Gains Amid Holiday Supply Concerns
By Masood Farivar
538 words
1 July 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Oil prices briefly topped $74 a barrel Friday in an extension of gains that have seen futures jump more than 7%
over the past two weeks.
The August crude contract on the New York Mercantile Exchange settled 41 cents higher at $73.93 a barrel, the
ninth straight gain. During the past two weeks, crude futures have advanced nearly $5, or 7.2%.
Front-month July gasoline futures, which gained nearly 30 cents since June 19, tumbled 9.27 cents to settle at
$2.2021 a gallon as traders liquidated long positions ahead of the contract's expiration.
"You can never predict expiration days," said Tom Bentz, an analyst at brokerage BNP Paribas Futures in New
York. "I guess long [positions] overstayed their welcome."
The gains were fueled by concerns about gasoline supplies going into the long Fourth of July holiday, which is
expected to see a record 34 million travelers hit the road.
Gasoline inventories fell about a week ago for the first time in more than two months and are expected to shrink
further over the summer as refiners grapple to meet rising summer demand.
U.S. refiners have faced an unusually high number of snags this year. Nearly two weeks ago, a key ship channel
in Louisiana was shut by an oil spill, cutting the crude supply to four refineries and forcing them to cut production.
The U.S. Coast Guard is conducting tests at least through Saturday to determine whether oil tankers will be able
to pass through without disrupting the cleanup effort.
"The market is totally focused on refinery and gasoline problems and the problems in the Middle East," said Chris
McCormack, an analyst at ABN Amro in New York.
The gains in crude futures came as commodity traders breathed a sigh of relief after the Federal Reserve
signaled Thursday that it might pause its two-and-a-half-year-long interest-rate-increase campaign. Fear of an
economic slowdown triggered by rising interest rates had weighed on commodities earlier in the month.
"The risk capital that went to the sidelines are finding their way back to the market," said Phil Flynn, an analyst at
Alaron Trading Corp. in Chicago.
The return of speculative money to the market could mean new record highs of more than $75 a barrel as early
as next week, said Mike Guido, director of commodity strategy for Societe Generale in New York. <para> But Mr.
Flynn said he wouldn't be surprised to first see a correction when markets open next Wednesday, "even though
longer term we'll go to [a record] $78."
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose as a drop in the U.S. dollar
combined with low market liquidity ahead of the Independence Day holiday to boost prices. Nearby July gold
futures gained $27 to $613.50 an ounce.
COFFEE: Prices on the New York Board of Trade soared as forecasts warned of a chance of cool weather in
Brazil's coffee-growing areas next week and speculative funds bought futures. July coffee rose two cents to 99.50
cents a pound.

Profit Reports Fuel Stock Rally --- Dow Climbs Above 11000 Mark And S&P 500 Is Back in Black Amid
Upbeat Corporate Results
By E.S. Browning
497 words
22 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
UPBEAT corporate-profit news helped spark a broad stock rally, sending the Dow Jones Industrial Average back
above 11000 and putting the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index back into positive territory for the year.
Following reports of strong quarterly results from delivery service FedEx Corp. and brokerage firm Morgan
Stanley, the Dow Jones Industrial Average surged 104.62 points, or 0.95%, to 11079.46, its highest finish since
June 2. The blue-chip average was up as much as 156.89 points at the day's high and suffered some profit-taking
at day's end, but it finished ahead 3.4% for the year. It is down 4.8% since hitting a six-year high on May 10.
The S&P 500, which has trailed the Dow lately and had been showing a decline for the year, rose 0.97%, or 12.08
points, to 1252.20. That ended a three-day losing streak and nudged the index into a gain of 0.3% for 2006.
On top of the profit news, stocks benefited from a broad rebound in commodity prices, with copper, oil and other
industrial commodities, as well as gold and silver, gaining ground. That helped reinforce hopes that the world
economy is strong enough to withstand expected global interest-rate increases, aimed at forestalling inflation.
Reflecting the economic optimism, the Dow Jones Transportation Average, which includes FedEx, surged 2.93%,
or 135.53 points, to 4765.51.
Small stocks and several foreign stock markets also showed gains.
Hopes spread among investors that stocks are in for a rebound after hitting multimonth lows early last week.
"People have a tendency to say we probably bottomed out last week," said Bob Basel, senior trader at Citigroup
Inc.'s New York brokerage arm. Investors are becoming optimistic about the prospects for quarterly profit reports,
due in large volume in the middle of next month. "But the market is still going to be held hostage to what the Fed
is going to do and what the next set of economic numbers are," he said.
Fed Reserve policy makers are expected to push short-term interest rates higher when they meet next
Wednesday and Thursday, and investors worry that the Fed could raise rates further this summer.
The Nasdaq Composite Index ended a three-day decline, rising 1.62%, or 34.14 points, to 2141.20. It is down
2.9% for 2006.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose; bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 63 cents for each $1,000 invested,
pushing the yield up to 5.159%. The 30-year bond was down 4/32 to yield 5.197%.
The dollar weakened to 114.80 yen, down from 114.93, as the euro rose to $1.2662 from $1.2583.

4.5% for the year.
Some economists are starting to predict that the Federal Reserve could raise its target for short-term interest
rates three more times this year, starting at the central bank's meeting of policymakers Wednesday and Thursday
of next week, to prevent inflation by slowing the economy. If the Fed continues its practice of quarter-point
increases each time, it would push the target rate to 5.75%, higher than most investors expected just a few weeks
ago. Rising rates crimp corporate profits, and uncertainty about how high they will go makes investors nervous
about owning stocks and other securities.
Yesterday, "investors used the opportunity to sell into the rally," which explains the weak finish, said stock trader
Larry Peruzzi at Boston Company Asset Management. He said he saw many late-day computer-driven trades,
suggesting that some investment pros have decided to use stock gains as an opportunity to pull back until the
outlook becomes clearer.
The day started on an upbeat note, with stocks rising after May housing starts came in stronger than expected,
even as new building permits fell. The data quieted fears about a sharp housing downturn, suggesting that any
real-estate decline is occurring at a moderate rate.
Foreign stocks were little changed on the day, as were oil futures. Gold futures, which have been under fire,
rebounded $8.10 to $576.90 a troy ounce.
Outside the U.S., stocks were unchanged in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S.
stocks, remained at 202.37.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed and bond prices fell. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or 63 cents for each $1,000
invested, pushing the yield up to 5.151%. The 30-year bond was down 4/32 to yield 5.189%.

In Dubai, Gold Sellers Grumble --- As Jewelry Prices Soar, Some Retailers Risk Bankruptcy
In Dubai, Gold Sellers Grumble --- As Jewelry Prices Soar, Some Retailers Risk Bankruptcy
By Elisabeth Behrmann
893 words
20 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
A karat is a unit of measure for the fineness of
gold, while a carat is a unit of weight for precious stones. An earlier version of
this article incorrectly used carat when referring to gold.
(WSJ Jan. 30, 2016)
Dubai, United Arab Emirates -- THE RISE IN GOLD PRICES has boosted investors' profits. But it isn't good news
for gold retailers in Dubai, the gold-trading hub of the Middle East and one of the world's biggest distribution
centers for the precious metal.
Dubai retailers are finding they can't sell their gold at the high price and are having trouble covering their loans.
Gold surged to $730.65 a troy ounce on May 12, at the time a rise of 41% for the year. While the price has since
receded below $600, it is still up 30% from a year ago and is likely to stay high as investors diversify their
portfolios against the threat of inflation and geopolitical tension.
Dubai has been a major crossing point on international trading routes in silk, pearls, spices and gold for the past
600 years, and its gold "souk," or marketplace, has emerged as a major tourist destination.
Most of the shops in the bustling marketplace are Indian-owned, offering gold jewelry at some of the lowest prices
in the world thanks to Dubai's free-trade policy and low taxes. Dubai accounts for about 20% of the global
physical gold trade, much of which moves on to India, and the emirate has the world's highest per-capita gold
consumption.
On a recent weekday, the souk was teeming with shoppers, but sales have fallen 30% to 35% following the spike
in prices on global exchanges, said Umesh Sagar of Nanjee Parshotam Jewellers, forcing the bankruptcy of "two
or three shops with many more in that queue."
"Prices at around $350 to $400 an ounce were good but now we have no choice but to pass on the increased
cost while adding a 5% to 8% risk premium because of the volatility," Mr. Sagar said.
As a result, jewelry prices have gone up by 25% to 28%.
Most of the retailers in Dubai use gold loans to finance wholesale gold purchases, mostly from Standard Bank
and Nova Scotia, according to Moaz Barakat, managing director for the Middle East, Turkey and Pakistan at the
World Gold Council.
The rise in gold prices has pushed the dealers' credit lines to the limit and increased their financing costs. But the
extreme volatility of the price over recent weeks has also pushed up margins for gold-futures hedges.
"One retailer was short and couldn't cover his positions. When the bank called to put margins down, he was
unable to do it, and the bank stepped in," one person familiar with the Dubai market said, declining to name the
retailer. Margins cover the maximum probable one-day move in the price of a futures contract.
Gold imports to Dubai totaled 525 metric tons during 2005, up some 20% from a year earlier, but first-quarter
figures for 2006 show a drop of 20% in imports from the same quarter in 2005 to 100 tons, according to figures
from the World Gold Council.

--- Page 187 ---
Page 187 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Some shops have been forced to go bankrupt while some are in big trouble," said Dilip Popley, managing
director of Popley Kewalram Jewellers LLC.
Kumar Dhanak, managing director of JM Jewels, said, "The hike in gold prices has affected the normal consumer
business. We've had a 30% to 35% drop in sales and we have to compromise on margins."
Gold at $400 an ounce allowed retailers a profit margin of 8% to 12%, but that has dropped to 5% or 6%, said Mr.
Dhanak, whose shop has been in business for 50 years.
Mr. Dhanak, who hedges some of his gold needs with his supplier, said he carries the "absolute minimum of
stock, down some 15% to 25% of what we normally have."
The gold souk in Dubai's Deira neighborhood, the oldest part of the city, caters mostly to the tastes of Western
tourists but also features shops selling the ornate designs and gold belts sought by customers from the Middle
East.
Most jewelry in Dubai's 700 jewelry shops is sold by weight rather than design or craftsmanship. There is also a
steady trade in 24-carat gold bars and 22- and 24-carat bullion.
"There's nothing we can do about the prices. Here the feeling is that funds have kidnapped the market and are
playing with sentiment," said Dilip Popley, managing director of Popley Kewalram Jewellers LLC. "It's the funds
taking advantage of the small size of the market. It's not the traders that have benefited."
Another headache for retailers in Dubai's gold souk are overhead costs as rents have shot up roughly 25% a year
for the past four years.
Dubai's government has had a 15% cap on rental increases in place since 2005, but other living expenses such
as food, education and fuel are also rising by double digits.
"The increase in cost of living is such that some people have moved back to India," said Mr. Sagar, who came to
Dubai from India in 1977.

Dollar Is Higher Versus Euro, Yen In Light Trading
Currency Trading
Dollar Is Higher Versus Euro, Yen In Light Trading
By Azam Ahmed
397 words
20 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar was up versus its rivals yesterday, maintaining gains won overnight in a day of light trading and no
data.
The yen fell sharply overnight as reports that North Korea was preparing for a long-range missile test sent the
Japanese currency to eight-week lows against the dollar and record lows versus the euro. The yen weakness
helped to push the dollar higher compared with the euro.
As the New York session got under way, the yen began to recover from its overnight losses versus the euro as
investors took profits, dropping the euro by almost an entire yen. The euro selloff helped support the dollar versus
the common currency.
"Lofty levels for the euro-yen pair . . . were sold off, which generally means a lower euro," said John McCarthy,
director of foreign exchange at ING in New York.
Late yesterday in New York, the euro was at $1.2577, compared with $1.2639 late Friday. The dollar was at
115.42 yen, from 115.13 yen late Friday, while the euro was at 145.16 yen, from 145.51 yen. The dollar was
quoted at 1.2410 Swiss francs, from 1.2309 Swiss francs, while sterling was at $1.8412, from $1.8498 late Friday.
"Most of the action took place overnight, where the excuse for bidding dollar/yen up" were the reports on the
North Korean missile-test preparations, said Grant Wilson, senior foreign-exchange trader at Mellon Bank in
Pittsburgh. "Some of that [weakness] spilled over into the euro currencies."
Also helping the dollar was a pullback in commodities, including gold, silver and oil. August gold settled down
$9.30 at $572.40 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, and July silver slipped
16 cents to $9.97 an ounce. July and August oil contracts traded under $70 a barrel.
Analysts said that the dollar has continued to benefit from increased expectations for rising interest rates in the
U.S. The marked is pricing the chance of a June Federal Reserve interest-rate increase at 100% and an August
rate rise at more than 70%.
This is particularly true as the European Central Bank disappointed markets at its meeting this month by sounding
less hawkish than many had anticipated.

Inflation Fears Hit Wall Street Again As S&P 500 Veers Back Into the Red
Monday's Markets
Inflation Fears Hit Wall Street Again As S&P 500 Veers Back Into the Red
By E.S. Browning
511 words
20 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STAGFLATION FEARS reared their heads again, knocking stocks down and sending the Standard &Poor's
500-stock index back into negative territory for 2006.
Worries that persistent inflation could make the Federal Reserve push interest rates too high, stifling economic
growth, sent the Dow Jones Industrial Average down 72.44 points, or 0.66%, to 10942.11, its ninth decline in 12
sessions. It is up 2.1% this year, but down 6% since hitting a six-year high May 10. Stagflation is the combination
of inflation and a slumping economy.
The S&P 500, however, fell back into the red for the year. It declined 0.91%, or 11.40 points, to 1240.14, off 0.7%
since the year began.
Stocks began the day with gains, following advances in several European and developing-country markets. But
then Jack Guynn, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Atlanta, repeated that inflation is too high, which
reinforced fears that the Fed could raise interest rates more than once this summer. Higher rates are usually bad
for stocks and bonds, because they crimp corporate profits and make existing bonds, with their lower rates, less
attractive.
Stocks fell further after the National Association of Home Builders reported that its index of business conditions
fell to its lowest level in 11 years. That reinforced fears that Fed rate increases could overshoot the mark, slowing
the economy too much and squeezing profits.
"The market has the perspective of a slowing economy, including a slowdown in housing, which certainly has
been the engine of growth in the last several years," said Jay Suskind, director of trading at brokerage firm Ryan
Beck &Co. in Florham Park, N.J. "It is tough to buy the market right now."
Investors were nervously awaiting today's report on May housing starts.
Oil futures continued to decline, falling 90 cents to $68.98 a barrel, but instead of reassuring investors, that news
reinforced concerns about economic weakness. Gold fell $9.20 to $568.80 a troy ounce.
The Nasdaq Composite Index decreased 0.92%, or 19.53 points, finishing at 2110.42, down 4.3% this year. Small
stocks led the declines.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, fell
0.53%, or 1.08 points, to 202.37.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.18 billion shares,
as 867 stocks rose and 2,461 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 3/32, or 94 cents for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
up to 5.143%. The 30-year bond was down 4/32 to yield 5.181%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 115.42 yen, up from 115.13, while the euro fell against the
dollar to $1.2577 from $1.2639.

Gold Holds On to Finish Higher; Recent Selloff Raises Concerns
Commodities Report
Gold Holds On to Finish Higher; Recent Selloff Raises Concerns
By Alison Guerriere Ciaccio
447 words
17 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures shrugged off steady gains in the U.S. dollar to end higher on Friday on the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, while market players argued whether a temporary bottom has
been set in the market after Tuesday's wild selloff.
The front-month June gold contract ended $11.50, or 2%, higher at $578 an ounce. The most-active August gold
contract settled $11.40 higher at $581.70. While gold held earlier gains during the day, it couldn't rally further as
the dollar bounced higher in reaction to the narrower U.S. current-account deficit.
Some choppiness was seen late in the session as the yellow metal came in danger of falling into negative
territory, but at the close gold climbed back near session highs, traders noted.
"Gold finished the week off on a plus note," said Bill O'Neill, a managing partner at Logic Advisors.
While some market players said the metal is showing signs of a temporary bottom, Mr. O'Neill said a
consolidation mode is playing out as traders await more evidence of a bottom. Tuesday, gold fell sharply in the
biggest dollar drop in 26 years as investors worried about global growth.
"We would have to see more follow-through on rallies, and there also needs to be evidence that the asset
diversification has concluded before we can say that a bottom is in place," said Mr. O'Neill.
Dave Rinehimer, head of futures research at Citigroup Global Markets, said gold appears to be struggling to
recover after Tuesday's losses.
"The market got overextended on Tuesday's decline and we are recovering, but it's not a robust recovery when
based on expectations related to inflation concerns, interest rates and global growth," said Mr. Rinehimer.
During Tuesday's selloff, August gold futures fell to a low of $565.50. Weakness in crude oil and the equities
markets along with strength in the dollar contributed to the selloff and rippled across commodity markets. Others
analysts said the downtrend for gold may not yet be over.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE: Futures edged modestly higher to just under $70, as traders bought back positions sold earlier in the
week on worries of a global economic slowdown. The July crude-oil contract settled 38 cents higher at $69.88 a
barrel.
NATURAL GAS: Futures fell slightly after a two-day gain of 17%, while contracts for months further in the future
indicated bullish sentiment. The July natural-gas contract settled 2.2 cents down at $7.185 a million British
thermal units.

Blue Chips Rally 198.27 to Return To Above 11000
Thursday's Markets
Blue Chips Rally 198.27 to Return To Above 11000
By E.S. Browning
989 words
16 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
REASSURING COMMENTS on inflation from Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke helped propel the Dow
Jones Industrial Average ahead almost 200 points, marking its biggest two-day gain in three years and pushing it
past the 11000 milestone again.
Although many on Wall Street remained nervous about the market's outlook, few were standing in its way
yesterday as surging markets abroad and good news at home spurred the buying.
The Dow industrials finished with a gain of 198.27 points, or 1.83%, at 11015.19. The Dow is ahead 2.8% for
2006, although still down 5.4% since its six-year high set May 10.
It was the biggest one-day gain in point or percentage terms since April 2005. Together with Wednesday's
111-point surge, the 2.89% two-session gain was the biggest since April 2003. All 30 Dow-industrials components
rose, the second time that has happened since early 2004. (The other was in April 2006.)
After all the losses, "this market was kind of like a rubber band. It got stretched too much on the downside and
snapped back," said Todd Clark, director of stock trading at San Francisco money-management and brokerage
firm Nollenberger Capital Partners. "Bernanke's comments about the inflation bugaboo also reassured people.
But the proof is going to be next week. What I don't want to see is another ungluing."
The day's big news was Mr. Bernanke's speech to the Economics Club of Chicago, in which he said inflation
expectations indicated by yields on Treasury bonds have "fallen back somewhat." He said energy prices so far
have had a "relatively low" impact on other prices.
Mr. Bernanke, who took over Fed leadership in February, gave no direct hints about his plans for interest-rate
increases this summer. Investors expect the Fed to push short-term rates up another quarter percentage point at
its policy meeting June 28 and 29. The comments suggested that Mr. Bernanke has a balanced view on inflation
risks, which renewed hopes for a pause in rate increases.
"Investors still are learning how to dance with Mr. Bernanke," said Jon Brorson, head of growth-stock investing at
money-management group Neuberger Berman. "But it sounds like he assuaged people's fears a little bit in
Chicago." Mr. Brorson said he is confident that Mr. Bernanke "isn't going to drive the economy into the ground."
Like Mr. Clark, Mr. Brorson said the rally was in part a technical reaction to the heavy selling of the past month.
He said he expects stocks to remain volatile, as investors try to understand the likely outlook for interest rates.
Some short-term investors could use the current rebound as an opportunity to sell some stocks they had trouble
unloading during the downdraft.
Even before Mr. Bernanke spoke, stocks began the day with gains, partly in reaction to sharp rallies abroad.
India's benchmark index jumped almost 7%, Tokyo's 1%, Russia's 5% and those of Britain and Germany 2%.
Colombia's soared almost 16%.
Some of the market swings could be due to the "quadruple-witching" expiration of options and futures contracts at
week's end. When stocks and indexes move sharply, they can make options and futures contracts pay off,
causing traders to buy or sell stocks to cover their exposure. When trading is distorted by short-term buying and
selling, market moves can be partly unwound in later days.

--- Page 197 ---
Page 197 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Some of the buying also could be by short sellers, who borrow stock and sell it in a bet that it will decline. To take
profits, they must buy stocks back and return them, which can temporarily contribute to market rallies after big
declines.
Investors welcomed a series of mixed economic reports, which together suggested that inflation isn't getting out of
control and that the economy is continuing to expand. Industrial production unexpectedly fell in May, as did the
pace of expansion of Philadelphia-area factory activity. May manufacturing activity in New York state was strong
and last week's new jobless claims declined. Brokerage firm Bear Stearns posted record quarterly profits.
Treasury prices, which had risen during the stock downdraft as money flowed to Treasurys' relative safety,
declined for a second day, pushing yields slightly higher. The 10-year Treasury note fell 10/32, or $3.13 for each
$1,000 invested, pushing the yield up to 5.104%. The 30-year bond was down 18/32 to yield 5.138%.
Oil futures rose 36 cents, but finished below $70 at $69.50 a barrel. Gold futures rebounded after seven
consecutive declines, rising $4.20 to $566.50 a troy ounce. Gold futures are down 21% since hitting a 26-year
high in May.
Investors are looking ahead to quarterly profit reports, due next month. Early forecasts of problems and triumphs
are starting to trickle out.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 2.12%, or 26.12 points, to 1256.16, the biggest percentage gain
since 2003. It is 5.2% off the five-year high it hit May 5, but back in the black for 2006, up 0.6%.
The Nasdaq Composite Index posted its biggest percentage gain since 2004, up 2.79%, or 58.15 points, at
2144.15. Although the Nasdaq has returned to its level of one week ago, it remains down 9.6% from its April 19
five-year high and off 2.8% since the year began.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index jumped 3.54% to 701.05, its biggest one-day gain since 2002. It is down
more than 10% from the record it hit in May.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.94%, or 3.84 points, to 201.71.
