A Threat to Metals Prices: Plastics --- A Shift by Manufacturers to Less-Expensive Copper Substitute Would Realign Demand
Commodities Report
A Threat to Metals Prices: Plastics --- A Shift by Manufacturers to Less-Expensive Copper Substitute
Would Realign Demand
By Patrick Barta
770 words
16 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Bangkok -- EVEN AFTER the recent pullback in metals prices, some market watchers see a further risk, and they
can sum it up in one word: plastics.
When prices for copper, nickel and other raw materials stay high as they have been until recently, big commodity
users often look for alternatives. These can include an array of substitutes -- notably plastics, but also a variety of
other materials -- that might not be as good as the preferred metal but cost a lot less.
Home builders can save money by substituting plastic pipes for copper plumbing. Makers of air-conditioning units
can replace copper coils with components made from less-expensive materials, such as aluminum.
Such substitution strategies aren't expected to add serious new downward pressure to commodity prices soon.
Despite the big drops in metals prices -- copper, for instance, has fallen about 15% over the past month amid
fears of higher interest rates and slower global growth -- most analysts expect commodity costs to remain above
their historical averages for some time.
That is because mining companies are struggling to develop new supplies, and demand in China, India and other
developing countries is expected to remain strong. At about $7,000 a metric ton, copper is still more than four
times its 2001 price and about 60% higher than at the start of the year.
With higher energy costs, prices for substitutes like plastics and aluminum have risen, too, but not nearly as much
as some other metals. Although plastics are often made with oil, raw materials make up a smaller percentage of
the cost of the final product than they do for items like copper pipe. Aluminum is up only about 65% since 2001, in
part because it faces fewer supply constraints than metals like copper.
Over the longer term, though, analysts say the "substitution" effect could produce some permanent changes to
the market that could have an impact on metals prices. If more raw-material users switch out of certain products,
particularly copper, it could result in much-lower-than-expected demand. That would allow mining companies to
catch up with demand more quickly -- and perhaps even mark the beginning of the end for the commodity boom.
CRU, a London commodity-research firm, recently concluded that the use of plastic pipes and other alternatives
cut copper demand by as much as 250,000 metric tons last year -- roughly 1% of world-wide consumption.
While 1% might not sound like much, it could make the difference between a market that has excess copper and
one in deficit.
CRU predicts there could be more substitution in other businesses ahead. It cites air-conditioning units, for
example, as a big source of demand for copper coils, used to transfer heat.
Johnson Controls Inc. , Milwaukee, has begun promoting a new type of unit that uses aluminum instead of
copper. Although the switch to aluminum was driven by other factors, including a desire to reduce the size of the
unit, it also cut costs. Analysts suggest Chinese manufacturers could follow suit -- a potentially important
development since Chinese demand for air-conditioning units has been red-hot.
If Chinese air-conditioner makers switch out of copper, "you could see some large losses [in demand] very
quickly," said Jon Barnes, an analyst at CRU.

--- Page 199 ---
Page 199 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Other metals analysts believe the threat to commodities markets from substitution is overstated. Many of the best
substitutes are expensive, these analysts note. Also, switching to different materials may require big investments
in new equipment.
Also, some replacement materials just don't work that well. In China last year, producers of stainless steel
decided that nickel, an important raw material, was getting too pricey. Producers began using less nickel and
more manganese, and global nickel prices fell more than 25%.
But it turned out that the new stainless-steel varieties weren't sufficiently weather-resistant. Chinese producers
began switching back to nickel this year, and the metal's price soared to a record in May.
In commodity trading yesterday:
CRUDE OIL: Futures rose for a second straight session as bargain-hunters returned to the market after two
sessions of steep losses. The July crude contract on the New York Mercantile Exchange rose 36 cents to $69.50
a barrel.
GOLD: Prices took part in a broad rally in commodities, in particular aided by stronger crude oil and stocks and a
softer dollar. On the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, June gold rose $4.20 an ounce to
$566.50.

As Inflation Signs Grow, Divergent Reactions --- Dow Gains More Than 110 Points Despite Worries of Rate Increase; Short Sellers, Boeing...
Wednesday's Markets
As Inflation Signs Grow, Divergent Reactions --- Dow Gains More Than 110 Points Despite Worries of Rate
Increase; Short Sellers, Boeing Likely Factors
By E.S. Browning
814 words
15 June 2006
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2006 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS REBOUNDED strongly despite more signs of inflation, leaving investors debating whether it was a
one-day snapback or something more lasting.
After falling in seven of the eight previous sessions, the Dow Jones Industrial Average surged 110.78 points, or
1.03%, to 10816.92. It swung back to a gain of 0.9% for 2006, after sinking into a decline for the year on Tuesday.
It still is down 7.1% from the six-year high hit May 10.
It is common for stocks to stage brief rallies in the middle of longer-term declines, often fueled by bearish
investors known as short sellers when they pause to take profits. To make their bets, short sellers borrow stock
and sell it. They hope to profit by buying it back and returning it after a decline. So, to take profits, short sellers
must buy stocks, pushing them temporarily higher. The question is how much of the rally was caused by short
sellers, and how much by longer-term buying.
More than a third of the Dow's gain, or 40.24 points, came from a single stock, Boeing Co., which rose $5.03, or
6.5%, to $82.01. Boeing won a big order, at the same time that rival Airbus announced delayed production for its
giant A380 jet.
Across the market, things that had been down were up, and vice versa. Several hard-hit foreign markets
rebounded. Japan recovered 0.6%, Russia 3%, Turkey almost 2%, Austria 1% and Colombia more than 6%.
India, China and Britain continued to fall, however.
Oil futures, which had been falling, rose 58 cents to $69.14 a barrel, although gold futures fell 20 cents to $562.30
a troy ounce.
Treasury bonds, which had risen as money flowed to their relative safety, fell heavily. That was partly because
money flowed back to stocks and partly because of the inflation jitters. The dollar, which had been surging, also
pulled back.
Inflation worries worsened after a government report showed May consumer prices, excluding volatile food and
energy prices, rising more than expected. The gain was above the 2% annual level that Federal Reserve officials
have said marks the limit of their comfort zone. That reinforced expectations that the Fed will push benchmark
short-term interest rates up another quarter point at its policy meeting June 28 and 29. The expectation of higher
rates makes investors less eager to own existing bonds, with their lower rates.
Reinforcing the expectation of higher rates, Dallas Federal Reserve Bank President Richard Fisher called current
inflation expectations "unacceptable."
The inflation news also was bad for stocks because it reinforced fears that the Fed could raise interest rates into
the summer, further weighing on corporate profits, consumer spending and economic growth. Ordinarily, that
concern would have pushed stocks down. Its failure to do so suggested that the stock gains were more a rebound
from the recent declines than a reaction to the day's news.
Overall, almost as many stocks fell as rose on the New York Stock Exchange. But traders said they were pleased
to see stocks rally strongly at day's end, instead of fading as they so often have lately.

--- Page 203 ---
Page 203 of 203 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"This market has endured significant damage in the last two weeks, and the selling isn't disappearing," said Andy
Brooks, head of stock trading at Baltimore money-management group T. Rowe Price. While there is no guarantee
that the declines won't resume, he said, "my own sense is that we are closer to having washed through this."
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.52%, or 6.35 points, to 1230.04, still down 1.5% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.65%, or 13.53 points, to 2086.00, down 5.4% for 2006.
Outside the U.S., stocks rose in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks, rose
0.67%, or 1.32 points, to 197.87.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. Total trading volume for stocks listed on the New York Stock Exchange was 2.77 billion shares;
1,757 stocks rose and 1,556 fell.
Bond prices declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 24/32, or $7.50 for each $1,000 invested, pushing the yield
up to 5.064%. The 30-year bond was down 1 7/32 to yield 5.100%.
The dollar weakened. Late in New York, it traded at 115.06 yen, down from 115.30, while the euro rose against
the dollar to $1.2593 from $1.2545.

Investors' Jitters Surface" -- WSJ June 15, 2006)
